The EU’s competition regulator yesterday slapped a  fine of 318.2 million euros (R3.7 billion) on the European wax division of petrochemicals producer Sasol for leading a price-fixing cartel dubbed the “paraffin  mafia”.

The penalty, payable in three months, is nearly 10 times the R381 million operating profit generated in the year to June 2008 by Sasol  Wax, the unit that houses the group’s Hamburg-based  European       wax operations.

TD
Sasol said in response that it was “surprised and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this fine”.
It would be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodging an appeal.
Sasol’s fine comprises nearly half of the total 676 million  euros penalty imposed on nine  cartel
participants. The others are:
n Exxon Mobil, the world’s biggest oil multinational;
n Italian oil and gas group ENI;
n German groups Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol and RWE;
n Hungarian oil producer MOL;
n Spanish oil group Repsol; and n French oil major Total.

Europe’s biggest oil group, Shell, avoided a  fine because it had disclosed the  cartel.
South Africa’s competition  commission said yesterday that it had received complaints against
Sasol’s South African  wax division for excessive pricing and exclusionary practices.
“We are looking into it,” said commissioner Shan Ramburuth. He said no formal investigation had yet been launched.

SE      Legislation/Regulation
HD     Brussels Fines 'Paraffin  Mafia' WC     192 words
PD      2 October 2008
SN      The Oil Daily
SC      TOILDA
LA      English
CY      (c) 2008 Energy Intelligence Group. All rights reserved.

LP
The European Commission has fined nine companies a total of €676 million ($946 million) for operating a price-fixing "paraffin  mafia."

The nine -- which include South Africa's Sasol, France's Total and US supermajor Exxon Mobil -- were found guilty of participating in a  paraffin wax cartel from 1992 to 2005 in the  European Economic Area in violation of  European Union rules.

TD
A 10th company, Royal Dutch Shell, had its fine waived after its tip-off led to surprise raids in April 2005 by the commission. Shell would have been fined €96 million.
The steepest €318.2 million fine was levied on Sasol, which the  commission identified as the
ring-leader, while Total was fined €128.2 million and Exxon €83.6 million. RWE was  fined €37.4 million,
Eni €29.1 million, Hansen & Rosenthal €24 million, Mol €23.7 million, Repsol YPF €19.8 million and
Tudapetrol €12 million.
Sasol's  fine would have been double had it not cooperated, while Eni's  fine was increased by 60%
because of its involvement in other cartels dating back to the 1980s (OD Dec.6,p7).

SE           News
HD           Sasol will appeal R3.7bn fine
BY           Oscar.Stuart
WC          342 words
PD           2 October 2008
SN           Daily News SC           DAILNW ED           e1
PG           15
LA           English
CY           © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
Sasol, the world’s big-gest maker of motor fuel from coal, said it would likely appeal the hefty  fine
imposed on it by the  European Commission for fixing prices.

The EC charged nine petrochemicals companies a total of E676 million (R7.9 billion) yesterday for forming a “paraffin  mafia” to fix prices and carve up markets for  paraffin wax.

TD
“There is probably not a household or company in  Europe that has not bought products affected by this ‘paraffin  mafia’ cartel, with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage,” EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said.

Paraffin waxes are used in a wide range of products from candles to paper cups and plates, the  wax
coating of cheese, chemicals, tyres and chewing gum.

The biggest individual  fine of E318.2 million was imposed on Sasol of South Africa and Germany, which the European Union executive described as the leader of the illegal  cartel, an official statement said.

Kroes said the  cartel was known inside Shell as the “paraffin  mafia” and in Sasol as “Blauer Saloon” (Blue Saloon) after the hotel bar in Hamburg, Germany, where the first meetings of executives from the companies were held.
It was a very serious infringement of EU treaty anti-trust rules, compounded by the duration of the
cartel, she said.

“Sasol is surprised by and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this  fine and will be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodge an appeal against it,” the company said in a statement.

Anglo-Dutch oil major Shell escaped a potential  fine of E96 million because it blew the whistle first to the EU competition authorities.

The Commission said the cartel activity took place bet-ween 1992 and 2005, when it began investigating with raids on petrochemicals companies prompted by an application from Shell for leniency in return for information provided.
The companies were well aware their activity was illegal, Kroes added.

LP
£535m price-fixing fine
A CARTEL of oil companies were yesterday  fined £535million for price-fixing.

TD
The European Commission described the 10 firms who took part as the continent's "ParaffinMafia".
They spent 13 years manipulating the price of  paraffin wax.

It has hundreds of uses, from packaging, adhesives and chewing gum to candles,  waxed paper and the coating on cheese.
EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said: "There is probably not a household or company in
Europe that has not bought products affected by this 'ParaffinMafia' cartel.

"Such illegal  cartel behaviour cannot and will not be tolerated by the  commission and companies' managers and shareholders should take note."

The commission investigation started with surprise inspections in April 2005, prompted by an application for immunity lodged by Shell under the EU's 2002 "leniency notice".
They escaped punishment yesterday.

The nine firms  fined were ENI, ExxonMobil, Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol, MOL, Repsol, Sasol, RWE and Total.

The first cartel meeting took place in Germany in 1992. Others were held at a series of top hotels in cities all over Europe, including Milan, Vienna, Budapest, Paris, Munich and Strasbourg.

The firms discussed prices, allocated markets and customers, and exchanged sensitive commercial information.
Kroes said the  cartel's activities were "a very serious infringement of EU Treaty anti-trust rules".

HD       European Commission imposed  fine on MOL WC     224 words
PD      2 October 2008

SC      ENPNEW

LP
Release date - 01102008

The European Commission has today fined MOL Euro 23.7 million for participating in an alleged concerted activity with regard to the European paraffin  market for the period of 1992-2005.

TD
As MOL has already stated while co-operating with the  Commission on this matter, MOL's position is that no official corporate decision was made with regard to participating in  cartel-type behaviour in case of the affected product.

Paraffin  sales represent a very small proportion, less than 0,5% of MOL's total revenues. The sales activity in this market was undertaken by an earlier acquired subsidiary of MOL in the beginning of the investigated period, and following that was taken over to the parent company's responsibility.

MOL regrets that in the 1990s it did not at that time have sufficient internal controlling and audit systems which could have identified and filtered out a situation such as that identified by the  Commission in its investigation.

MOL has yet to study the final decision and has a period of two months in which to reflect on the findings of the decision and in order to determine whether to lodge an appeal with the Court of First Instance in Luxembourg.

HD          Majors hit by $1 billion EU fine over paraffin cartel
BY           Robert Perkins
WC          610 words
PD           2 October 2008
SN           Platts Oilgram News
SC           PON
PG          1
VOL        ISSN: 0163-1284, Volume 86, Issue 195
LA           English
CY           (c) 2008 McGraw-Hill, Inc.

LP
The European Commission has fined nine companies including ExxonMobil, Eni and Total more than Eur676 million ($959 million) for operating a  price fixing cartel on paraffin wax headed by South African refiner Sasol.

The Commission said that from 1992 to 2005, the producers of  paraffin waxes and its feedstock slack wax in the European Economic Area operated a "paraffin  mafia" in which they fixed prices for paraffin  waxes.

TD
ExxonMobil, Hungary's MOL, Spain's Repsol YPF, Sasol, Shell and Total engaged in market allocation for paraffin  and ExxonMobil, Sasol, Shell, Germany's RWE and Total also fixed prices for slack  wax sold to end-customers on the German market.

The biggest  fine of over Eur318 million was handed out to South African refiner Sasol, which saw its penalty increased by 50% because it was the leader of the  cartel.
ExxonMobil received an a  fine of Eur83.5 million, with smaller penalties going to fellow  cartel
members Repsol, Italy's Eni, Germany's Tudapetrol, Hansen & Rosenthal and RWE, Total and MOL.

Shell escaped any  fine under the EU's whistle-blower rule, which applies to companies that help the EU competition authorities uncover a  cartel. Eni's fine of Eur29.1 million was increased by 60% as it had previously taken part in similar cartels, the Commission said.

"There is probably not a household or company in  Europe that has not bought products affected by this 'paraffin  mafia'  cartel, with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage," EU Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said in a statement.

"Such illegal  cartel behavior cannot and will not be tolerated by the  Commission, and companies' managers and shareholders should take note."

Sasol said it was "surprised" by the ruling and is considering grounds to appeal its  fine, which must be paid within three months. "Sasol is surprised by and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this  fine and will be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodging an appeal against it," the company said in a statement.

Sasol said it was unaware of the  cartel infringements before the  Commission began its investigation into the  wax business in Hamburg in April 2005.

In a statement, ExxonMobil said it "deeply regrets" its involvement in the  cartel, adding the employees concerned no longer work at the company.

Despite Sasol's base  fine being increased by 50% to reflect its leadership of the  cartel, the Commission said it later reduced the  fine by the same amount in a leniency move as the refiner had cooperated with its investigation.


Page 83 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Paraffin waxes are used in a wide variety of products such as candles,  waxed paper, paper cups and plates, the wax coating on cheese, chemicals, tires and car components as well as in the rubber, packaging, adhesive and chewing gum industries. The  European market is worth almost Eur500 million/year.

The Commission launched its investigation with a round of surprise inspections in April 2005, prompted by notification of the  cartel and by an application for immunity lodged by Shell.

The Commission said the companies involved held regular meetings to discuss prices, allocate markets and/or customers and to exchange sensitive commercial information.
Within Shell, the  cartel was referred to as the "paraffin  mafia" and in the Sasol group, as the "Blauer
Salon" (blue saloon), after a hotel bar in Germany where the first meetings of the  cartel took place.

Subsequent meetings took place at a series of top hotels all over  Europe, including Milan, Vienna, Budapest, Paris, Munich and Strasbourg, the  Commission said.

HD     RENEWED CALL FOR SASOL RE-NATIONALISATION WC    486 words
PD     2 October 2008
SN     SAPA (South African Press Association)
SC     SAPA
LA      English
CY     (c) 2008 All copy held by SAPA, no republication without permission from SAPA

LP
News of Sasol's R3,7 billion  fine for price fixing  and market sharing  led to renewed calls for the renationalisation of the chemical and fuel company on Thursday.

Sasol was fined about R3,68 billion (Euro 6,7 million) on Wednesday by the European Commission for being a leader of a cartel, which, with eight other companies, was found to have fixed prices for paraffin wax products in Europe.

TD
"The EU  fine once more underlines the correctness of the SACP's call for the re-nationalisation of this strategically critical corporation," said the SA Communist Party of the company which was privatised in
1979.

It argued that because Sasol produces about 30 percent of local oil needs, it is an extremely important national strategic asset.

The party also claimed the company charges the same price as an imported product for products it manufactures locally.
"... as if Sasol oil had come all the way from the Middle East," it commented.

They called for intervention by the SA Competition  Commission and the introduction of a windfall tax on the company's oil profits which would be invested in energy security and public transport.
The Competition  Commission's Shan Ramburuth said the structure of the market for the supply of
paraffin wax is different in South Africa from that of the  European Union.
"In fact in South Africa, Sasol is dominant in the supply of  wax and the collusion does not arise," he said. "However, participants in this market have raised concerns with the  commission with regard to Sasol's
pricing and exclusionary conduct. The  commission is looking into these concerns."

A commission tribunal previously prohibited Sasol's purchase of Price's candles because of exclusionary conduct, but the company successfully appealed this and bought the company.

The commission recently completed an investigation into Sasol's role in an investigation into the fertiliser industry. The company provides one of the inputs for fertiliser. This will be heard before a tribunal shortly.

Company spokesman Johann van Rheede said the company would not comment further until it had read the full documentation related to the  fine.
On Wednesday it announced that it may appeal as it did not understand why the  fine was so big.
The company did not respond to a question on whether it could afford to pay the  fine (over three months)
or if anyone had resigned or been fired as a direct result of the  commission's decision.
Cosatu's Patrick Craven said the latest "scandal" reinforced their call for the nationalisation of Sasol.

Paraffin waxes are used in a wide variety of products such as candles,  waxed paper, paper cups and plates, the wax coating on cheese, chemicals, tyres and car components as well as in the rubber, packaging, adhesive and chewing gum industries. The market is worth almost 500 million  euros, the European Commission said.
Sapa

PSYCHOLOGICAL FREQUENCY 'Urgent' need for Brown to take more risks

AS IF things couldn't get any worse for him, it is only a matter of time before risk-averse Gordon Brown completely loses the confidence of his colleagues and country, according to a leading human resources and management consultant.

Bridget Biggar, managing director of Management Intelligence Consulting, says the troubled prime minister's "psychological signals" are "on completely the wrong frequency", despite some respite in attacks on his "dithering" since the Labour Party conference.

Biggar says there is possibly a way back for him, but only if he goes completely against his psychological make-up - and starts to take risks and bring urgency into his personality.

She adds: "Ironically, he's actually overplaying his strengths, he's trying too hard - in doing so, he is moving from productive use of those strengths to excessive, causing a sure and insidious fall off in credibility.

"As chancellor to Tony Blair, his risk-averse nature was disguised by Blair's high-energy and human approach. But Brown's personality traits and approach have shot to the fore since he entered Number
10."

SE           Business
HD           'Paraffin  mafia' comes unstuck after Euro 676m fines
BY           Rory Watson, Brussels
WC          366 words
PD           2 October 2008
SN           The Times
SC           T
PG           55
LA           English
CY           © 2008 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP
The European Commission imposed one of the highest cartel fines in its history yesterday as it penalised nine companies for operating an illegal "paraffin  mafia" for more than 13years.

The combined penalties of Euro 676 million (Pounds 534 million) on the companies for fixing prices and carving up markets for paraffin  wax - which is used in products such as candles, tyres and adhesives - are the latest sign of the Commission's determination to stamp out cartels that prevent customers benefiting from competition.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the EU Competition Commissioner, said: "There is probably not a household or company in Europe that has not bought products affected by this 'paraffin  mafia'  cartel, with all that implies in paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage."

The Commission made dawn raids on the companies three years ago after Shell, one of its members, blew the whistle on the illegal activities.

The investigators found that the companies held regular meetings to discuss prices, allocate markets and customers and exchange sensitive commercial information.

Shell escaped a possible  fine because it provided the  Commission investigators with the initial vital information.

Sasol, the South African-German company that was considered the leader of the group, was  fined      Euro 318.2 million. Among its employees, the  cartel was dubbed the Blauer Salon (Blue Saloon) after the hotel bar in Germany where the first meetings were held.

Sasol, the world's biggest manufacturer of motor fuel from coal said: "Sasol is surprised by and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this  fine and will be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodge an appeal against it."
The other companies  fined were Total of France (Euro 128.2 million), ExxonMobil of the US (Euro
83.6 million), RWE of Germany (Euro 37.4 million), ENI of Italy (Euro 29.1 million), Hansen &
Rosenthal of Germany (Euro 24 million), MOL of Hungary (Euro 23.7 million), Repsol of Spain (Euro
19.8 million) and Tudapetrol of Germany (Euro 12 million).
The global paraffin wax market is worth almost  Euro 500 million. (c) Times Newspapers Ltd, 2008

HD          Sasol faces R3,7bn EU price-fixing penalty
BY          Adrian Ephraim
WC         286 words
PD          2 October 2008
SN          The Star SC          THESTR ED          e1
PG          1
LA           English
CY          © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
The European Union’s competition regulator has slapped a  fine of e318,2-million (about R3,7-billion) on the European wax division of petrochemicals producer Sasol for leading a  price-fixing cartel dubbed the “paraffin  mafia”.

The penalty, payable in three months, is nearly 10 times the R381-million operating profit generated in the year to June 2008 by Sasol  Wax, the unit that houses the group’s Hamburg-based  European wax operations.

TD
Sasol said in response yesterday that it was “surprised and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this  fine”.
It would be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodging an appeal.

Sasol’s fine comprises nearly half of the total e676-million penalty imposed on nine  cartel participants. The other guilty parties are:
nExxon Mobil, the world’s biggest oil multinational. nItalian oil and gas group ENI.
nGerman groups Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol and RWE. nHungarian oil producer MOL.
nSpanish oil group Repsol.
nFrench oil major Total.

Europe’s biggest oil group, Shell, avoided a  fine because it had disclosed the  cartel.
South Africa’s Competition  Commission said yesterday it had received complaints against Sasol’s
South African  wax division for excessive pricing and exclusionary practices.

“We are looking into it,” said commissioner Shan Ramburuth, adding that no formal investigation had yet been launched.

Because Sasol is the dominant producer of  wax in South Africa, competition issues revolve less around collusion than dominance. The Competition Tribunal prohibited a merger between Sasol and candle-making group Prices Daelite several years ago, but this was subsequently overturned by the Competition Appeal Court.

HD    EU shows what it means to be a competition watchdog
BY    Glenn Dyer writes:
WC   424 words
PD    2 October 2008
ET     05:09 AM
SN    Crikey SC    CRIKAU LA     English
CY    (c) Copyright 2008 Private Media Partners Pty. Ltd. All Right Reserved.

LP
The European Union issued a massive fine overnight against nine companies for  cartel-behaviour. Could be something in it for Australia's competition watchdog. By Glenn Dyer.
The Australian's Matthew Stevens
(http://www.theaustralian.news.com.au/story/0,25197,24433025-5001641,00.html) took a swipe at the
European Union's investigation of the BHP Billiton bid for Rio Tinto. From what he wrote, he seems to
think the EU doesn't know vey much about competition or things like cartels,  price fixing  and the like.
Unlike the ACCC with its long and impeccable track record of victories?

TD
Well, Matthew should have a chat to the ACCC who will tell him that the  Europeans  are the nasty folk of world trade and anti-cartel behaviour. They are the heavy guys of global competition authorities and take the whole idea of competition very seriously indeed.
(http://europa
.eu/rapid/pressReleasesAction.do?reference=IP/08/1434&type=HTML&aged=0&language=EN&guiLangua
ge=en) And overnight they scored a huge win over a claimed  cartel that fixed the price of  wax used in
candles, plates and paper cups.

It doesn't sound much, but the  fine was huge -- 676 million euros, close to $A1.2 billion, depending on exchange rates. That was the fourth highest  fine from the EU ever (
(http://europa
.eu/rapid/pressReleasesAction.do?reference=MEMO/08/600&format=HTML&aged=0&language=EN&guiL
anguage=en) check this list for all-time list). The companies involved were Exxon, Total, ENI, Saso1
(which was fined 318 million euros and the fourth highest individual  fine ever) and five others. Nine
companies in total. Sasol's fine was boosted by 50% because it was the leader of the pack.
The Commission said the cartel ran from 1992 to 2005.

They might be a bunch of  European public servants, but they don't scare easily, and as the link to the website shows, have levied some enormous  fines in major industries and against some of the world's biggest companies.

So they won't be pressured or threatened by lobbying by BHP, Rio or  European steel groups, or by BHP's supporters in Melbourne.

HD      COMPETITION : COMMISSION FINES NINE COMPANIES OVER PARAFFIN WAX CARTEL WC     424 words
PD      2 October 2008
SN      Europolitics SC      EURREP VOL    3608
LA      English
CY      Copyright 2008 Europe Information Service All Rights Reserved

LP
The European Commission announced, on 1 October, that it had  fined nine paraffin wax producers a total of €676 million for sharing the  European market and fixing prices for 13 years.

For the two biggest  fines, Sasol (Germany, South Africa) will have to pay €318.2 million and the French company Total €128.1 million. Then ENI (Italy) will have to pay €29.1 million; the American company ExxonMobil €83.5 million; German companies Hansen & Rosenthal €24 million; Tudapetrol €12 million; RWE €37.4 million; the Hungarian company MOL €23.7 million and the Spanish company Repsol €19.8 million.

TD
Paraffin waxes are used in products such as candles,  waxed paper, paper cups and plates, the  wax coating on cheese, chemicals, tyres and car components as well as in the rubber, packaging, adhesive and chewing gum industries. The  European market is worth almost €500 million.

A tenth company involved in the cartel, Shell (UK-Netherlands), was exempted from paying any  fine as it was the first to denounce the cartel to the EU executive. "Shell and its shareholders did the right thing," commented Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes, knowing that, lacking this denunciation, the petrol giant would have had to settle a bill for €96 million. Other discounts were granted to Sasol, Repsol and ExxonMobil. These three groups benefit from a reduction of their  fine of 50%, 25% and 7%, respectively, for having cooperated in the  Commission's investigation, as set out in its leniency programme adopted in
2002.

The meetings between the incriminated parties took place at a series of top hotels all over  Europe, including Milan, Vienna, Budapest, Paris, Munich and Strasbourg. The infringements committed by ExxonMobil, Sasol, Shell, RWE and Total also concerned slack  wax - a raw material used in the production of  paraffin wax - sold to end-customers on the German market.
In the Shell group, the  cartel was called the paraffin  mafia', this illegal  cartel prospered from 1992 to
2005. It was also called Blauer Salon' (blue saloon) in the Sasol group, after a hotel bar in Germany
where the first meetings of the  cartel took place.

The fine will take fourth place among the highest financial sanctions inflicted by the  Commission against corporate cartels, after elevators (€992.3 million in 2007), vitamins (€790.5 million in 2001) and
switchgear (€750.5 million in 2007). Kroes toughened the rules in 2006 to sanction cartels more heavily, by taking better account of the duration of the  cartel and repeat offences.

SE          Guardian Financial Pages
HD         Financial: 'Paraffin  mafia' firms given pounds  500m fines for price-fixing
BY          Terry Macalister
WC         415 words
PD          2 October 2008
SN          The Guardian
SC          GRDN
PG         29
LA          English
CY          © Copyright 2008. The Guardian. All rights reserved.

LP
A fine of more than pounds 500m has been slapped on a group of leading energy companies including
ExxonMobil and npower's parent, RWE, after the whistle was blown on a  price-fixing cartel by Shell.

The penalty, one of the highest  cartel fines imposed by the European commission, resulted from secret meetings which began at a saloon bar in Germany. This led to the fixing of prices and markets for everyday products containing  paraffin wax such as chewing gum, tyres and candles.

TD
Neelie Kroes, the  European commissioner for competition, said the leader of the "paraffin  mafia" was South Africa's Sasol but it included other leading oil companies. "I hope the harsh lesson of these high fines will encourage the management of these companies to look very carefully at what their staff is doing," said Kroes. "And I hope that in turn the shareholders of these companies look very carefully at the management."
The European market for paraffin wax totals about  euros 500m (pounds 397m) a year and the  cartel
covered about three-quarters of that between 1992 and 2005, added Kroes.
The companies had shown a common resolve to rig the market when they started meeting in
Hamburg's Blauer Salon under the code-name "the Blue Saloon group".

Meetings followed all over  Europe as the cartel developed, but Shell began informing the  commission about the illegal practices after discovering the role being played by DEA, a subsidiary it acquired from RWE in 2002. A Shell spokeswoman in London said last night it had been "disappointed" to uncover
the market abuse while the  commission said the Anglo-Dutch group's cooperation had helped it avoid a potential  euros 96m fine.

The other companies involved can appeal, and Sasol said it intended to. "Sasol is surprised by, and does not understand, the reasons for the magnitude of this  fine and will be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to an appeal," it said. ExxonMobil was uncertain on its course of action but said in a statement it "deeply regrets its involvement, although limited, in the infringement of competition laws through the participation of a few of the company's former employees".
France's Total was  fined euros 128m and

ExxonMobileuros 83.5m. Others penalised were Repsol of Spain, Italy's Eni, Tudapetrol, Hansen & Rosenthal and RWE, all of Germany, and Moll in Hungary.

HD     Handelsblatt: Hans-Otto Schumann  alleged to have been involved in wax cartel. WC    235 words
PD     2 October 2008
SN     Handelsblatt
SC     HB
PG     15
LA      English
CY     The Financial Times Limited. European Intelligence Wire. All material subject to copyright. Handelsblatt ©
2008 All rights reserved.

LP
The German entrepreneur Hans-Otto Schumann is alleged to have been involved in the case relating to
an alleged paraffin wax cartel. According to EU authorities, a  cartel for market-sharing and price-fixing
was formed in 1992, before his  wax firm, Hans-Otto Schumann GmbH & Co, was acquired by the South
African oil and chemicals group Sasol. Even following the change of ownership in 2003, Mr Schumann is
reported to have remained active in business until 2005, the year in which the  price-fixing is said to have
ended.

TD
The Anglo-Dutch oil and gas group Shell is reported to have discovered the alleged  cartel in 2005, around three years after its acquisition of the German oil company DEA from energy group RWE. The EU Commission is reported to have demanded fines from 10 companies, including 318m  euros from Sasol as owner of the Schumann company and alleged cartel leader. The German company Hansen & Rosenthal, which is among the companies facing fines, plans to take legal action. Its management has denied having participated in a  cartel, claiming that the agreements related, not to higher, but to lower prices, with the aim of forcing small companies, such as Hansen & Rosenthal, from the market.

(ADP News) - Oct 2, 2008 - Hungarian oil and gas company MOL (BDP:MOL), along with eight other petrochemical companies, was  fined EUR 23.7 million (USD 33m) by the  European Commission (EC) for cartel activities between 1992 and 2005, Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes said Wednesday.

EC fined the cartel, having a 75% share of the European paraffin-wax market, with a total of EUR 676 million, which is the fourth biggest fine imposed by the EC. The cartel included 10 companies, but Dutch Shell (NYSE:RDS.A) was not fined, because it was the company to reveal the  cartel to the Commission. Apart from MOL other  fined companies are South Africa’s Sasol (JSE:SOL), France’s Total (PAR:FP), US-based Exxon Mobil (NYSE:XOM), Italy’s Eni (BIT:ENI), RWE of Germany (FRA:RWE), Germany’s Hansen & Rosenthal, Respol of Spain and Tudapetrol of Germany.

TD
PETROCHEMICALS The European Commission fined nine petrochemicals companies a total of
€676m (£534m) yesterday for forming a "paraffin mafia" to fix prices and carve up markets for paraffin  
wax, although Shell escaped a €96m fine because it blew the whistle. "There is probably not a
household or company in Europe that has not bought products affected by this 'paraffin  mafia'  cartel,
with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage," the EU
Competition Commissioner, Neelie Kroes, said.  Paraffin waxes are used in products from candles to
paper cups and plates, the  wax coating of cheese, chemicals, tyres and chewing gum. Ms Kroes said the
cartel was known inside Shell as the "paraffin  mafia" and in Sasol as "Blauer Salon" (Blue Saloon) after
the hotel bar in Hamburg, Germany, where the first meetings of executives from the companies were
held. It was the fourth-highest  fine imposed by EU regulators on a sector. The firms  fined were Exxon
Mobil, Repsol, ENI, Tudapetrol, Hansen & Rosenthal, RWE, Total and MOL.

LP
The European Union imposed one of its highest  cartel fines, accusing nine companies of fixing prices and markets for  paraffin wax, used in products like chewing gum, tires and candles.

Sasol Ltd., ExxonMobil Corp., Eni SpA, Hansen &Rosenthal, Tudapetrol, MOL Nyrt, Repsol SA, RWE AG and Total SA will have to pay a total of 676 million euros ($951.6 million). South Africa's Sasol was fined the most -- 318 million  euros -- said the European Commission, the EU's executive arm.

TD

HD        €676m fine for Paraffin Mafia
BY         GEOFF MEADE
WC       239 words
PD        2 October 2008
SN        Metro SC        METRO ED        DUBA PG        1
LA         English
CY        (c) 2008 Associated Newspapers. All rights reserved.

LP
THE European Commission has imposed fines of €676million on a  Paraffin  Mafia that for years ensured every household in Europe paid too much for goods.

Leading companies, including Total, Exxon-Mobil, Resol, Sasol and Shell, were involved in an illegal paraffin wax scam, operating a price-fixing, market-rigging  cartel which ran for 13 years until 2005. Shell alone escaped fines under Commission leniency provisions because the company revealed the existence of the cartel and claimed immunity.

TD
EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes told a press conference in Brussels: There is probably not a household or company in Europe that has not bought products affected by this  Paraffin  Mafia cartel, with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage.

Such illegal cartel behaviour cannot and will not be tolerated by the  Commission, and companies managers and shareholders should take note, added Ms Kroes.

The Commission investigation started with surprise inspections in April 2005, prompted by an application for immunity lodged by Shell under the EUs 2002 Leniency Notice.

According to the  Commission, the cartel held meetings in top  European hotels to discuss and fix prices, allocate markets and customers, and to exchange sensitive commercial information.Paraffin      wax is used in candles, waxed paper, paper cups and plates, as well as in chemicals, tyres, car components and in the rubber, packaging, adhesive and chewing gum industries.

HD          Sasol fine ‘won’t  affect SA consumers’ BY          ella.smook
WC         582 words
PD          2 October 2008
SN          Cape Argus SC          MEWCAP ED          e1
PG          3
LA           English
CY          © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
Synfuel giant assures local market
By ELLA SMOOK

TD
Staff Reporter

South African synfuel giant Sasol has given its assurance that the  price-fixing which led to its German operation receiving a mammoth  fine had not impacted on local consumers.

“This is a European matter, and is not impacting on any local product,” Sasol spokes-man Johann van Rheede told the Cape Argus yesterday, after it emerged that the  European Commission had imposed a e318.2 million  fine on the company.

Sasol had received the lion’s share of a total fine of e676m imposed on nine international groups for participating in a  paraffin wax cartel in the European Economic Area.

The company could also face further claims, as firms and individuals affected by its anti-competitive behaviour may seek damages in the courts of member states, while the finding by the  commission is binding proof that the illegal behaviour had occurred.

Shell, which had also participated in the  cartel, was not fined because it had been the whistleblower on the cartel.

Other companies who received  fines, all of them payable within three months, were Repsol (Spain), ExxonMobil (US), ENI (Italy), Tuda-petrol, Hansen&Rosenthal and RWE (Germany), MOL (Hungary) and Total (France).
In a statement released by Sasol in South Africa yesterday, the company said it had ex-pected the
commission’s finding.

“As anticipated in several previous announcements made by Sasol Limited, the  European Union found that members of the  European paraffin wax industry, including Sasol  Wax GmbH, had formed a
cartel and violated antitrust laws.”

Its co-operation with and support of the  commission’s in-vestigations had led to a reduction of 50% in the base amount of the  fine to e250m, Sasol and the  commission said.

However, Sasol added that it had been “surprised by” and did not understand the reasons for the magnitude of the  fine, and would be studying the reasons for the ruling with a view to lodge an appeal against it.

The commission said in its press release that it had taken into account the “respective affected sales of the companies involved, as well as the combined market share and the geographical scope of the

Page 106 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
cartel agreements” in determining the size of  fines and that Sasol’s  fine had been increased by 50%
because it had been the  cartel leader.
Asked whether it would be investigating Sasol locally in light of the  commission ruling, the
Competition  Commission of South Africa said Sasol’s dominant position in the country’s  paraffin wax
market meant there would not be a collusion case against it locally.

However, concerns had been raised with the  commission that Sasol might be abusing its dominant position by employing excessive pricing and exclusionary behaviour, the  commission added.

Following the  European Commission’s finding, Sasol said yesterday that the in-fringement of antitrust laws had begun “in 1992 or even earlier”, while it had became a co-shareholder in the implicated German wax business only in 1995.

It had acquired the remaining shares in the joint venture in 2002 to become the sole shareholder of the business.

“Sasol Limited was un-aware of these infringements before the  European Commission commenced their investigation at the  wax business in Hamburg in April 2005,” the statement explains.

Paraffin waxes, as distinct from liquid  paraffin  used in many South African households, are used in products such as candles,  wax paper, packaging, tyres and car components.

SE           Business Report
HD           ‘Mafioso’ Sasol fined E318m
BY           Mzwandile Faniso
WC          599 words
PD           2 October 2008
SN           The Star SC           THESTR ED           e1
PG           1
LA           English
CY           © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
The EU’s competition regulator yesterday slapped a  fine of €318.2 million (R3.7 billion) on the European wax division of petrochemicals producer Sasol for leading a  price-fixing cartel dubbed the “paraffin  mafia”.

The penalty, payable in three months, is nearly 10 times the R381 million operating profit generated in the year to June by Sasol  Wax, the unit that houses the group’s Hamburg-based  European wax operations.

TD
Sasol said in response that it was “surprised and does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this fine”.
It would be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodging an appeal.

Sasol’s fine comprises nearly half of the total €676 million penalty imposed on nine  cartel participants. The others are:
n ExxonMobil, the world’s biggest oil multinational;
n Italian oil and gas group ENI;
n German groups Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol and RWE;
n Hungarian oil producer MOL;
n Spanish oil group Repsol; and n French oil major Total.

Europe’s biggest oil group, Shell, avoided a  fine because it had disclosed the  cartel.
South Africa’s competition  commission said yesterday that it had received complaints against Sasol’s
South African  wax division for excessive pricing and exclusionary practices.

“We are looking into it,” said commissioner Shan Ramburuth, adding that no formal investigation had yet been launched.

Because Sasol is the dominant producer of  wax in South Africa, competition issues revolve less around collusion than dominance.

The competition tribunal prohibited a merger between Sasol and  candle making group Prices Daelite several years ago, but this was subsequently overturned by the competition appeal court.

In Europe yesterday, competition commissioner Neelie Kroes said in a statement that the penalty against Sasol was increased by 50 percent because it had led the  cartel.
Page 108 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
However, because Sasol was among four companies that had co-operated with the  commission in terms of its leniency programme, the company was also granted a reduction of 50 percent.

Kroes said: “In setting the  fines, the commission took into account the respective affected sales of the companies involved as well as the combined market share and the geographical scope of the cartel agreements.”

Sasol was cited for fixing prices in markets for paraffin  wax (which is used to make candles, the wax coating on cheese and chewing gum) and slack wax, the raw material used to manufacture paraffin wax. It was also found to have allocated markets and customers for  paraffin waxes.

The commission said that the  cartel members had held regular meetings to discuss prices, allocate markets and customers, and exchange sensitive commercial information from 1992 to 2005.

The cartel was referred to as both the paraffin  mafia and Blauer Salon (Blue Saloon), after a hotel bar in Germany where the first meetings of the cartel took place. Subsequent meetings took place in top European hotels, in cities ranging from Milan to Vienna.
Sasol said in a statement that it had become a co-investor in an existing  wax business owned by the
Schumann group in 1995, and then became the sole shareholder in the business seven years later.
It was “unaware” of the infringements before the  European Commission began its investigation in
2005.

“Sasol views this matter in a serious light, and has intensified its competition and antitrust law compliance programmes in all its businesses, including joint ventures,” said the group.

Sasol was listed last week on the Dow Jones sustainability index, whose criteria include corporate governance.
On the JSE, Sasol shed 5.1 percent to close at R332.

HD      Steer Clear of Cartels [editorial] WC     381 words
PD      3 October 2008
ET       09:32 AM SN      All Africa SC      AFNWS LA       English
CY      (c) 2008 AllAfrica, All Rights Reserved

LP
Johannesburg, Oct 03, 2008 (Business Day/All Africa Global Media via COMTEX) -- SASOL's costly experience with the  European competition authorities illustrates yet again just how high are the risks for SA companies going into joint ventures abroad.
The European Commission has fined Sasol EUR 318m for its participation in a nine-member  cartel
that fixed prices and parcelled out markets in  Europe's paraffin wax industry from 1992 to 2005.

TD
Sasol's share is almost half of the total of EUR 676m in fines to be paid by the nine, which include oil majors such as Total, Exxon Mobil and Shell. The commission upped Sasol's  fine 50% on the grounds that it was the  cartel leader but then cut it 50% because Sasol was the second company (after Shell) to come forward to blow the whistle on the  cartel. That enabled it to take advantage of the corporate leniency policy under which whistle-blowers gain immunity from prosecution.

Even so, Sasol's fine was horribly high, especially given that the turnover of its entire  wax business (in SA and in Europe) in the latest year was less than R1bn. The experience must be all the more bitter because Sasol gained full control of the Hamburg-based  wax business only in 2002, after it bought out its joint venture partner in the business, Schumann  Wax. The joint venture dated back to 1995, not long after the cartel began. But cartels are secretive by their very nature, not to mention illegal, so it was not likely the executives involved were going to tell all to their new shareholder. And joint ventures are anyway only a partial form of control.

Either way, Sasol clearly didn't know what was going on; when it found out in 2005, it immediately offered to co-operate with the  European commission. But too late, it seems, to avoid a steep  fine.
It probably is a lesson for all South African companies about the risks of offshore ventures, especially
joint ventures. The  wax case is also a reminder, yet again, of the efficacy of corporate leniency policies in
catching cartels. SA's Competition  Commission has also made good use of these. Nothing should be
done to undermine the effectiveness of these policies.

HD       Holding  on to Our Sasol Shares [column] BY       by Ben Temkin
WC      570 words
PD       3 October 2008
ET        09:37 AM SN       All Africa SC       AFNWS LA        English
CY       (c) 2008 AllAfrica, All Rights Reserved

LP
Johannesburg, Oct 03, 2008 (Business Day/All Africa Global Media via COMTEX) -- THE news on Wednesday of the huge  fine imposed on Sasol by the  European Commission more than soured Jean's and my day. It is an unpleasant feeling to know that some managers in the group were involved in a cartel.

Tiger Brands was one of the first investments we made for the Private Investor portfolio. When we learnt that some of its managers had been involved in fixing the price of bread, we did not immediately sell the shares. It later became clear, however, that the company's economic crime was not an isolated one. It also seemed that senior executives -- by not acting against the illicit practices -- had tacitly approved them.

TD
Our loss of confidence in Tiger Brands' management, rather than the exposure of the economic crimes, was the main reason for selling the shares. The exodus of CEO Nick Dennis and several other senior managers left a void. Their successors could be better or worse managers but we did not want to ride along while we found out.

Our past experience told Jean and I that a change in a company's leadership was often a poor investment fundamental.

I don't need to reiterate the history of the  paraffin wax cartel to confirm that Sasol's senior management inherited the damage and did their best to limit it. We will stick to CE Pat Davies and his team and hold our Sasol shares.

Of course, Sasol's earnings growth will be affected by the amount of the  fine, or -- what seems to be a faint hope -- a smaller amount. I suspect that the market has already more than discounted the effect of this in the share price.

In due course I plan to return to my review of Sanlam. However, before I do this, as I also received yesterday the monthly broker's statement on the Private Investor portfolio, it is timely to report on the portfolio's progress.

Last month, the portfolio received two dividends, a handsome one of R278,80 from Grindrod and a less handsome one of R74,73 from Anglo American. After administration charges and interest, the portfolio now holds cash of R2148,10. This cash amount is still far below the ideal critical mass for reinvestment.

The portfolio's index at the end of the month was 90,86, a paper loss of 9,14% since we seeded the portfolio with R100000 of our own money on August 21 last year.

Against most of the standard investment benchmarks, this is fair performance, but, as you know, we don't use benchmarks -- we assess the portfolio on the intrinsic investment fundamentals of the constituent counters.

We accept, though, that overall market sentiment apart, the share price weakness of one or more the counters may be telling us there is an investment fundamental about which we know little or nothing.


Page 111 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
It is disappointing that only three counters, Murray & Roberts, NewGold and Sasol, have shown paper gains. The remainder have paper losses varying from Hudaco's 2,13% to Bell's 49,5%.

On the investment fundamentals I know about, all the investments with paper losses to date look under-priced. Try as I want to I cannot, however, be either a disinterested or entirely objective commentator on shares I own.

South African petrochemical giant Sasol said Friday it would pay a 318-million-euro fine for its role in a
paraffin wax cartel but planned to appeal the  European Commission's judgment.
Sasol was handed the biggest fine of a total 676 million  euros (936 million dollars) levied by the
commission Wednesday against paraffin  wax producers including Exxonmobil and Total.

TD
"We will provide for the full amount of the  fine," chief executive Pat Davies told a media briefing. "The provisions will be immediately put in place for the current financial year."

Davies said the money would be raised from Sasol's internal treasury and the  fine should not hamper future growth due the company's strong balance sheet and cash flow.

"Fortunately Sasol is a successful company," he said. "Given our financial strength, we don't see this impacting on our future growth plans."

While Sasol admitted there was a  cartel, Davies said the company currently intended to appeal the findings but was waiting for the detailed reasoning from the  commission.

which has been dubbed the "paraffin  mafia."

"We have a difference of opinion there," he said, adding that it was the biggest producer of the group of companies.

South African petrochemical giant Sasol said Friday it would pay a 318-million-euro fine from the
European Commission for its role in a  paraffin wax cartel but plans to appeal the penalty.
Sasol was handed the biggest fine of a total 676 million  euros (936 million dollars) levied by the
commission Wednesday against paraffin  wax producers including Exxonmobil and Total.

TD
"We will provide for the full amount of the  fine," chief executive Pat Davies told a media briefing. "The provisions will be immediately put in place for the current financial year."

Grounds to appeal would be decided once the company receives the  commission's detailed reasonings, Davies said.
"We admit that there was a  cartel and we admit that some of our employees were involved in part of the
cartel. So obviously we cannot appeal against that," he said.

"Clearly the magnitude of the fine is one of things that we want to understand. If we find that we have no grounds of appeal to take on the fine, then we won't appeal. As we see things now, we intend to appeal."

Davies said the money would be raised from Sasol's internal treasury and the  fine should not hamper future growth due the company's strong balance sheet and cash flow.

"Fortunately Sasol is a successful company," he said. "Given our financial strength, we don't see this impacting on our future growth plans."
Davies said the company did not agree with the  commission's labelling it as the leader of the  cartel
which has been dubbed the "paraffin  mafia."

"We have a difference of opinion there," he said, adding that it was the biggest producer of the group of companies.

The European Commission found that members of  Europes's paraffin wax industry, including Sasol, formed a cartel and violated antitrust laws.
The fine of 318.2 million  euros is payable within three months.

"We regret that this has occured in one of Sasol's subsidiaries and ... failed to identify this anti-competitive behaviour," Davies said.

Shell escaped a fine under the whistle-blower rule, which applies to companies that help EU competition authorities to uncover a  cartel.
The fine for Sasol was increased by 50 percent "because it was the leader of the  cartel," the
commission said.

US giant ExxonMobil received an 83.5-million-euro fine with smaller penalties going to fellow  cartel members Repsol of Spain, Italy's ENI, Tudapetrol, Hansen and Rosenthaland and RWE of Germany, French Total and MOL of Hungary.

HD     Sasol Limited  (JSE: SOL; NYSE: SSL): Sasol Responds  Further to European Commission Fine
WC    881 words
PD     3 October 2008
ET      02:13 PM
SN     PR Newswire (U.S.)
SC     PRN
LA      English
CY     Copyright © 2008 PR Newswire Association LLC. All Rights Reserved.

LP
JOHANNESBURG, South Africa, Oct. 3 /PRNewswire-FirstCall/ -- This week, the  European Union found that members of the  European paraffin wax industry, including Sasol  Wax GmbH, formed a  cartel and violated competition law.

"We regret that this has occurred in one of Sasol's subsidiaries and that our due diligence and compliance programmes failed to identify this anti-competitive behaviour, when we acquired the business 13 years
ago and thereafter," said Sasol chief executive, Pat Davies.

TD
"Sasol is a successful business that is built on a foundation of over 30,000 committed and responsible employees. The past actions of but a few former employees has resulted in serious damage to our company's reputation and that affects each one of us," he said.

Sasol's management, including the Sasol directors of the Schumann-Sasol joint venture, was totally unaware of these activities. When we became aware of them in 2005, they were immediately stopped and Sasol promptly began cooperating with the  European Commission's (EC) investigation.

These cartel activities were clearly reprehensible and constituted a flagrant breach of Sasol's policies and values. By their very nature, these activities are secretive and very difficult to detect.

While a fine was expected, we didn't anticipate the magnitude of the  fine, particularly in the light of, amongst other factors, not being the legacy owner. We do not yet have the detailed reasoning from the EC and will study it, as soon as it is supplied, to clarify our next steps. As we see things now, it is our intention to appeal.

Sasol wishes to assure our shareholders and the broader public of the serious light in which matters of this nature are viewed.

We have rigorous compliance programmes relating to competition law. Clearly this is an area in which we must continuously improve as our business becomes more complex and competition laws around the world become more stringent. We have recently further intensified our compliance programmes with a particular focus on high risk areas such as joint ventures.

"We are on a journey of transforming the organisation and our values are central to this journey. These values define the manner in which we choose to conduct our business. The activities that led to this  fine are completely counter to the way we are transforming this organisation.

We wish to assure our employees, shareholders and our customers that there is no place at Sasol for illegal activities and we will vigorously root out any impropriety," Davies said.

During first week of Oct 2008, the  European Union found that members of the  European paraffin wax industry, including Sasol  Wax GmbH, formed a  cartel and violated competition law. Sasol's management, including the Sasol directors of the Schumann-Sasol joint venture, was totally unaware of these activities. When it became aware of them in 2005, they were immediately stopped and Sasol promptly began cooperating with the  European Commission's (EC) investigation. These  cartel activities were clearly reprehensible and constituted a flagrant breach of Sasol's policies and values. By their very nature, these activities are secretive and very difficult to detect. The company wishes to assure its employees, shareholders and customers that there is no place at Sasol for illegal activities and it will vigorously root out any impropriety. Based in South Africa and operating worldwide, Sasol is an integrated oil and gas company with substantial chemical interests.

HD     SASOL TO APPEAL FINE WC     230 words
PD      3 October 2008
SN      SAPA (South African Press Association)
SC      SAPA
LA      English
CY      (c) 2008 All copy held by SAPA, no republication without permission from SAPA

LP
Sasol on Friday accepted accountability for the anti-competitive behaviour that led to an approximately R3 billion fine for paraffin wax price fixing  in Europe, but it intended to appeal.

"I need to make it very clear that we don't distance ourself from accountability on this," said Sasol chief executive officer Pat Davies.

TD
However, the company would like to get details on how the European Commission (EC) concluded the matter to establish whether it was indeed a leader in the nine company cartel as alleged, and to see whether the size of the  fine was appropriate.

Davies said that as soon as the company was told of an investigation into cartels in April 2005, it immediately stopped it.

He said the company has over 30,000 employees and those implicated amounted to just a few individuals.
They had clearly breached company policy and values when they did this, he said.
They had co-operated with the  commission and were surprised by the magnitude of the  fine.

In terms of EC rules, they will pay the  fine over the next three months, probably through a combination of cash and borrowing.
"As we see things now it is our intention to appeal," he said.

The company had intensified its compliance monitoring and said that: "this is something that has happened in the past".

HD     SASOL TO APPEAL FINE WC     485 words
PD      3 October 2008
SN      SAPA (South African Press Association)
SC      SAPA
LA      English
CY      (c) 2008 All copy held by SAPA, no republication without permission from SAPA

LP
Sasol will pay the R3 billion  fine imposed on it by the  European Commission for price-fixing by its
German paraffin wax subsidiary, but also plans to appeal, chief executive Pat Davies said on Friday.
"We have to pay first and appeal later. This is the rule," Davies said in Johannesburg.

TD
Although the company expected the  fine after being informed of an investigation in 2005 into the activities of Sasol  Wax in Germany and those of nine other global companies dating back to 1992, it did not anticipate the magnitude of the  fine, said Davies.

It had co-operated with the  commission and immediately stopped cartel activities and was surprised by the Euro 318 million fine -- the fourth largest the EC's competition commission has imposed.

He said that although the company accepted that some staffers were involved in  cartel activities, it questioned the labelling of Sasol as the  cartel leader.
"I was not aware that they thought we were the leaders of the  cartel," he said.

Sasol hoped to get more details on how the  commission came to this conclusion when the final report was received.

It had not made provision for the  fine when told it was being investigated, because it had not known how much the eventual  fine would be.

The company would now probably provide for the  fine from the its internal treasury. Speaking on the sidelines of the briefing, Davies said Sasol might also borrow some money.

He said the company did not distance itself from accountability, but was not aware of this activity when it bought into the "legacy" company Schumann in the late 1990s and finally became its sole owner with a rebranding in 2002.
"I need to make it very clear that we don't distance ourselves from accountability on this," he said. Participating in the  cartel had been a "flagrant breach" of company policy and values and was profoundly
regretted, but its secretive nature had made it difficult to detect.
"We certainly would not have acquired this business had we know what was going on inside it," he said. Davies said that as soon as the company was told of the investigation by the  commission, it immediately
stopped the  cartel and issued a notice on the Stock Exchange News Service to inform shareholders.

The company has over 30,000 employees. It was only a few individuals who had damaged the reputation of the company and they had since "moved on".

"They are honest people, they are hard working people.... it's a handful of people that were involved in this," he said

The company had intensified its compliance programmes and would focus on high risk areas like joint ventures.

The other companies involved were: Shell, which was not  fined because it reported the activities to the competition authorities, Total, Exxon Mobil, RWE, ENI, Hansen and Rosenthal, Repsol and Tudapetrol.

JOHANNESBURG (I-Net Bridge)--Sasol Ltd. (SSL), the world's largest producer of motor fuels from coal, Friday said it will immediately make provisions for a EUR318.3 million (3.8 billion rand)  fine imposed by the European Commission.

Europe's antitrust watchdog this week  fined the Johannesburg-based company for its part in a
paraffin  wax cartel and violating antitrust laws.

TD
"We were unable to provide for it financially because we did not know the extent of the  fine," Chief Executive Pat Davies said, adding the company "certainly did not anticipate the magnitude of the fine".
Davies said the money will come from Sasol's internal treasury.

"We are fortunate to be a strong cash flow generator and have a strong balance sheet," he said, adding the fine will impact the operating profit line but isn't expected to have any impact on the company's expansion plans.

SE         Business Report
HD         How to survive  financial woes: drink and recycle
BY         Dineo Mamonyane
WC        964 words
PD         3 October 2008
SN         The Star SC         THESTR ED         e1
PG         2
LA         English
CY         © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
The following is the salutary story, doing the rounds in the UK, of how best to survive in the current tough environment.

The “advice” says that if a year ago you had purchased a stake worth £1 000 (R15 000 at yesterday’s exchange rate) in Northern Rock, the UK bank taken under state ownership in February, it would now be worth £4.95.

TD
If you had invested in HBOS, Britain’s biggest home loan lender, which the UK government arranged to be taken over by rival Lloyds TSB to avoid a collapse, then earlier this week your £1 000 would have been worth £16.50. And your £1 000 invested in XL Leisure, the British tour operator that collapsed last month, would now be worth less than £5.

But if you bought £1 000 worth of Tennents Lager one year ago, drank it all, then took the empty cans to an aluminium recycling plant, you would get £214.
“So, based on the above statistics the best current investment advice is to drink heavily and recycle,”
the advice concludes.

For those passionate about the environment and sustainable investment, it’s encouraging to see that the best investment advice these days has an environmentally friendly dimension to it. This investment suggestion also gives a different perspective on what constitutes rubbish.
Who’s fooling whom?

“We didn’t know.” This appears to be Sasol’s moral defence after one of its European subsidiaries was outed this week as the ringleader of a  price-fixing cartel in markets for paraffin  waxes.
At first glance a e318 million (R3.7 billion)  fine, which ranks at the top end of those yet imposed by the
EU’s antitrust watchdog, appears to be harsh punishment for the petrochemicals producer.

After all, Sasol says, it bought a stake in the German  wax business three years after the first meeting of the cartel took place in the Blauer Salon (Blue Saloon) hotel bar in Germany in 1992. And it took control of the business a decade after that first meeting.

Pleading ignorance certainly won’t let Sasol off the hook from paying the  fine imposed by the European Commission’s competition regulator, which, like South African competition authorities, has been cracking the whip on cartels in recent years.
Perhaps this line of defence is meant to restore confidence in Sasol’s corporate governance culture.

But there, too, it falls short. Just ask the Tiger Brands board, which is attempting to rebuild a semblance of corporate integrity after claiming to be unaware about price rigging at two subsidiaries, for which it incurred large penalties from the South African competition  commission.

Page 124 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
But the Tiger  fines take on the dimensions of Mickey Mouse compared with the jumbo-sized Sasol penalty.

The fact that the local watchdog has received several complaints against Sasol for alleged abuse of dominance at home does its image no good either.
While the  European case has given Sasol a profile as a “Mafioso”, local matters paint a
not-altogether-different picture of a bully – a case of “David versus Goliath”, in the words of one South
African complainant, Profert, a small fertiliser business.
It claimed that Sasol gave discriminatory price discounts to a competitor and refused to supply Profert. Sasol has also been accused of charging excessive ammonia prices by fertiliser producer Nutri-Flo, and
giving a competitor price and supply preferences.

There have been complaints about the group’s local  wax unit charging excessive prices and conducting exclusionary practices.

And of course, there is the case in which Nationwide Poles complained about Sasol charging its rivals lower prices for creosote. It won the case but subsequently had to close shop when Sasol won an appeal.
Government covers its bets

It is interesting and ironic that the government is doing some introspection on its performance regarding the gambling industry.
It is interesting because it is unusual for the government to be proactive and question its own decisions;
this is a responsibility that opposition parties have assumed.
The government wants to investigate if it has allowed too much gambling in the 12 years that it has
been legalised. This may not be answered; it depends on whether the proposal to set up a  commission
to investigate this will receive a green light.

In South Africa there have always been two extreme views on gambling. One sees it as a social evil that should be banned; the other supports it as a harmless form of entertainment. The government has tried to find common ground to accommodate both views. It constantly talks about the damage done by gambling, such as addiction. At the same time it praises the industry for its contribution to job creation, taxes and tourism.

In April, Rob Davies, the deputy minister of trade and industry, said a study commissioned by the National Gambling Board of SA in 2003 ranked the country 14th out of 50 nations based on gross gambling turnover. South Africa ranked higher than the Netherlands, New Zealand, Sweden and Singapore.

The irony in the government’s position is that before the industry was legalised, another  commission, headed by Nic Wiehahn, was established. That  commission found that if gambling was not permitted,
it would lead to an underground sector that would leave betters at the mercy of unscrupulous operators.

The government denies that it wants to review the Wiehahn recommendations, but it does want to assess if any activities should be curtailed.

While introspection is welcome, any decision that could lead to job losses in a country that is battling to win the war on unemployment must be approached carefully.
Edited by Samantha Enslin-Payne. With contributions by Ann Crotty, Ingi Salgado and Slindile Khanyile

SE      News
HD      BELGIUM: EC fines paraffin wax 'cartel'  players. BY      just-auto.com editorial team
WC     326 words
PD      3 October 2008
SN      Just-Auto SC      JUAUT LA     English
CY      © 2008 Aroq Limited. All rights reserved

LP
Some of Europe’s largest companies - including fuel majors - have been  fined heavily for participating in a paraffin  wax cartel, raising the cost of this key input for tyre and car component manufacturers.

Sasol, Total, ExxonMobil and six others were penalised a total of EUR676m for participating in what has been branded the 'paraffin wax mafia'.

TD
Members, the European Commission (EC) concluded, exchanged sensitive commercial information, fixing European Union (EU) paraffin  wax prices between 1992 and 2005, with executives meeting in luxury hotels across Europe in cities such as Milan, Vienna, Budapest, Paris, Munich and Strasbourg.
"There is probably not a household or company in  Europe that has not bought products affected by this
'paraffin  mafia'  cartel, with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic
damage," said EU competition commissioner Neelie Kroes, announcing the  fines.

She valued the EU  paraffin wax market at EUR500m a year; it is also a key input of most rubber goods, adhesives and chemicals also used by the auto industry.

The companies involved - and their  fines - were: Sasol (Germany) - EUR318m; Total (France) - EUR128m; ExxonMobil - EUR83m; RWE (Germany) - EUR37m; ENI (Italy) - EUR29m; Hansen & Rosenthal (Germany) - EUR24m; MOL (Hungary) - EUR23m; Repsol (Spain) - EUR19m; and Tudapetrol (Germany) – EUR12m.

Shell was also involved in the  cartel, but its EUR96m  fine was cancelled by the  commission because it tipped off anti-trust officials about the  cartel, leading to a series of raids on company premises in 2005.

As well as price fixing,  ExxonMobil, MOL, Repsol, Sasol, Shell and Total also agreed market allocations for the product, while ExxonMobil, Sasol, Shell, RWE and Total also fixed prices for slack  wax (paraffin   wax’s raw material) to end users in Germany.

This article was originally published on just-auto.com on 3 October 2008. For authoritative and timely auto business information visit  http://www.just-auto.com.

BUDAPEST. OCTOBER 3. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE - Hungary recorded a foreign trade deficit of EUR 365.1 mln in July 2008, up from the preliminary EUR 239 mln and widening from the EUR 115 mln deficit recorded in July 2007, according to final and detailed figures published by the Central Statistical Office (KSH) on Friday.

The July deficit came after Hungary noted a surplus for five consecutive months, which also reduced the year-to-date surplus to EUR 100.9 mln, which compares to the EUR 280.7 mln deficit noted in
January-July last year

TD
Hungary's exports reached EUR 6.11 bln in July, rising 4% year-on-year (y/y), while import volume was up 8% y/y to EUR 6.48 bln. In the first seven months of 2008, exports increased by 10% y/y, while imports were up 11% y/y to a respective EUR 43.84 bln and EUR 43.74 bln.
KSH figures indicate that Hungary's exports in the first seven months increased at above-average rates
of more than 30% y/y to Asian and non-EU  European countries. By contrast, EU-bound exports were up
just 9.7% y/y, including 5.9% y/y growth in exports to the original 15 member states.

BUDAPEST. OCTOBER 1. INTERFAX CENTRAL EUROPE – Hungary's top oil and gas company MOL has received a EUR 23 mln fine from the European Commission (EC), as part of a  cartel investigation involving the EU's paraffin  wax market that led to fines totaling EUR 676 mln.

"The cartel covered about 75% of the European market in paraffin wax, lasted for 13 years, and included 10 companies," Neelie Kroes, European Commissioner for Competition Policy announced in Brussels Wednesday. "The companies have been  fined a total of EUR 676 mln, the fourth largest  fine ever imposed by the EC in a  cartel case, [reflecting] both the long duration of the  cartel and the size of the market concerned – nearly EUR 500 mln a year."

Kroes said the  paraffin  mafia cartel ran from 1992 to 2005. In the beginning, the  cartel met at the Blauer Salon, a hotel bar in Hamburg, Germany.  Cartel meetings subsequently took place at a series of top hotels all over Europe – Milan, Vienna, Budapest, Paris, Munich, Strasbourg and others, according to the Commissioner.
German-South African manufacturer Sasol was the leader of the  cartel, and was rewarded with a EUR
318 mln fine. France's Total received a EUR 128 mln fine, while U.S.-based Exxon Mobil will have to pay
a EUR 83 mln penalty. Other companies involved in the cartel – including MOL – were awarded  fines
between EUR 12–37 mln each.
U.K.-based oil major Shell, which blew the whistle on the  cartel, has escaped what would have been a
EUR 96 mln fine, Kroes added.

Paraffin wax is used in a wide range of products – tyres, car components,  waxed paper, paper cups and plates, packaging, adhesive, candles, chewing gum, and the  wax coating on cheese, among others.

BUDAPEST. SEPTEMBER 30. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE – Hungary's current account deficit was EUR 1.84 bln in the second quarter of 2008, narrowing from EUR 1.97 bln in Q2 2007, but exceeding analyst expectations, figures released Tuesday by Hungary's central bank MNB indicate.

Page 128 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
In the first half of the year, Hungary's current account deficit was EUR 3.49 bln, opening up slightly from
EUR 3.41 bln in the same period of 2007.

In a year-on-year comparison, Hungary's second-quarter trade balance in goods and services improved by EUR 160 mln to a EUR 588 mln surplus. This was slightly offset by a EUR 28 mln increase in Hungary's income and transfer deficit to EUR 2.04 bln, thanks to higher income outflow on debt, and an increase in miscellaneous transfers.

Hungary's "net financing requirement," which also takes into account transfers received from the EU, was EUR 1.72 bln in Q2, down from EUR 1.83 bln a year earlier and amounting to 4.4% of GDP, according to seasonally-adjusted figures.

Non-debt generating financing of the current account deficit reached a negative EUR 1.02 bln in Q2, led by a large outflow of portfolio investments, the MNB said. Non-debt generating financing inflow in the first half of the year was EUR 550 mln, compared to a large EUR 3.8 bln outflow in the same period last year.

MNB figures also reveal that Hungary's gross foreign debt was EUR 89.9 bln at the end of the quarter, up EUR 4.6 bln from the previous quarter, thanks mainly to an increase in private sector debt. Hungary's net foreign debt was EUR 48.3 bln at the end of Q2, rising EUR 1.0 bln from Q1 thanks to an identical increase in public sector debt.

BUDAPEST. SEPTEMBER 29. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE – Hungary's draft 2009 budget, officially submitted to Parliament on Monday, counts on a cash-flow deficit of HUF 975.9 bln, with total expenditures of HUF 18,064 bln and total revenues of HUF 17,088 bln, Finance Minister Janos Veres said Monday, according to state news agency MTI.
The planned 2009 cash-flow deficit comes to 3.4% of GDP, compared to this year's projected 3.8%. The
2009 deficit according to the EU's ESA methodology, used to determine compliance with  eurozone entry
criteria, is seen at 3.2% of GDP, down from this year's 4.0% target.

Compared to a primary deficit – the balance of the budget minus interest payments on state debt – of HUF 103 bln this year, Hungary's state budget calculates with a primary surplus of HUF 199 bln, or 0.7% of GDP in 2009, Veres said.

Following plenary and committee debates in Parliament, a final vote on Hungary's 2009 budget is expected on December 15.

BUDAPEST. SEPTEMBER 29. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE – (adds quotes, detail) The Monetary Policy Council (MPC) of Hungary's Central Bank (MNB) decided at its Monday meeting to keep the base rate unchanged at 8.50%, meeting analyst expectations.

"In view of the significant increase in global uncertainty, as well as inflation risks that point in both directions, the Council decided to hold the base rate unchanged," MNB President Andras Simor told reporters, presenting the Council's statement.

The decision follows near-unanimous rate-hold decisions in each of the past three months, and analysts had widely expected the trend to continue in September, thanks to largely stable inflation developments and a strong currency.

"As in our previous meeting, we discussed two options, either keeping rates unchanged or a cut by 25 basis points, and an overwhelming majority voted in favor of holding rates," Simor said. "In the past month, inflation has developed in line with what we had anticipated in our [quarterly] inflation report in August."
Citing the Council's statement, Simor said that the U.S. financial crisis is having a mixed impact on the
Hungarian economy and its growth and inflation prospects.

Among others, stricter credit conditions and a slowdown in U.S. and  European growth could negatively impact growth in Hungary, while such a global slowdown could also help disinflation through lower energy, food and raw material prices. At the same time, global uncertainty could lower risk appetite and increase the risk premium on Hungarian assets, the statement added.

Simor added that despite the above effects, the financial crisis has had "no direct and significant impact" on Hungary's economy.

Commenting on local trends influencing inflation, the Council's statement notes that slow GDP growth has helped keep inflation in check. However, private-sector wage growth has been faster than what would be justified by productivity growth.

Page 129 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"Costs incurred by the real economy related to pushing down inflation could be eased if wage agreements were in line with the MNB's inflation target," the Council said.

Consumer price inflation in Hungary was 6.8% year on year (y/y) in the first eight months of this year, compared to the central bank's medium-term inflation target of 3%. Meanwhile, gross wages in Hungary were up 8.1% y/y in January–July, including 9.7% y/y in the private sector.

BUDAPEST. SEPTEMBER 29. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE – Hungary's average unemployment rate was 7.5% in the three-month period spanning June to August 2008, the same as in the May–July period but up 0.3 percentage points (pp) from the 7.2% unemployment rate in the same period one year ago, the Central Statistical Office (KSH) said Monday.

There were 317,200 unemployed people in Hungary during the period, up 12,600 on a year earlier and rising 1,500 compared to May–July. At the same time, the number of employed people fell by some
31,100 over the past year, to 3.914 million.

Hungary's labor-force participation rate, defined as the percentage of economically active individuals (whether employed or seeking work) within the 15–64 age group, fell by 0.3 percentage points on the year to 61.8%. Hungary's employment rate was 57.1% in this age group.

Hungary's unemployment rate harmonized in line with  Eurostat  methodology was 7.5% in July, the middle month of the three-month period, compared to 6.7% in the EU-15 and 6.6% across all 27 EU members, according to the KSH.

"After a long procedure to get the permits, which took nearly four years, we now have all necessary authorizations to start the HUF 20–25 bln investment in the first part of 2009," Stalmayer said.
