Stalmayer could not yet provide details of the financing plan of the project, but noted that Alba Airport expects to involve outside investors as well.

"We are in the process of finding other investors, such as property developers or professional investors, and although the credit crunch will surely impede this process, this project is good enough to attract other companies," Stalmayer noted.

The company sees Alba Airport as possibly the first genuine rival to Hungary's main international airport, Ferihegy near Budapest. While Ferihegy handles more than eight million passengers a year, most small regional airports in Hungary have rarely managed beyond a few tens of thousands of passengers a year.

"We expect to already have 150,000 passengers in 2010, while the capacity of the airport is eventually seen to be expanded to two million," Stalmayer said, noting that he expects to reach one million passengers by the fifth year of operation.

Stalmayer said that although the airport initially plans to serve low-cost airlines and charter flights, the facility will be able to accommodate traditional airlines as well.

Stalmayer noted that he is optimistic regarding the airport's growth potential because of its advantageous location, close to major tourist destinations Budapest, lake Balaton and lake Velencei, which are seen attracting a sizable influx of foreign tourists. Meanwhile, plans to create direct bus and train services to
the airport are aimed at maximizing the airport's domestic catchment area.

"The airport can be reached in a 90-minute drive by 3.5 million potential passengers, and this is essential in order to bring a discount airline base here," Stalmayer said.

He added that a low-cost airline base is expected to be opened at Alba Airport by the third or fourth year of operation.

In addition to creating new jobs locally, Alba Airport is also seen boosting the regional economy, by increasing tourist activity as well as bringing in industries that involve significant moves in personnel, such as the automotive and pharmaceutical industries.

Stalmayer noted that the company also plans to set up an industrial park on a 110-hectare plot near the airport, which is expected to attract additional passengers to the airport.

BUDAPEST. SEPTEMBER 30. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE – State-owned Hungarian electricity holding MVM and Matrai Eromu, a power plant operator owned by Germany's RWE-EnBW consortium, signed an agreement on Tuesday setting up a joint venture to build a HUF 200-bln, 400-MW lignite-fired block on Matrai's existing power plant site near Northern Hungary's Visonta.

"The new power plant will reduce Hungary's energy dependence and will definitely lower the average price of locally produced electricity," MVM CEO Imre Martha told reporters. "Building more power plants like this is inevitable."

MVM will own 74.9% of the joint venture, while Matrai will own a 25.1% stake. According to the agreement, MVM will be responsible for the construction of the power plant and for organizing the sale of electricity produced. Meanwhile, Matrai – which owns large lignite mines nearby – will be responsible for operating the power plant and securing fuel supplies.

The new 400-MW lignite-fired block, to be built between 2010 and 2015, will replace two 100-MW blocks that came online in the 1960s, part of more than 900 MW of total capacity at the power plant.
Compared to the 26% efficiency ratio of the outgoing blocks, the new block will have efficiency of 42%.
As a result of this and other technological improvements, the CO2 emission of the new block is estimated
at 0.86 kilograms per kWh of electricity produced, as opposed to the 1.22 kg emitted by the existing
blocks as of 2005. When measured together with the remaining blocks, Matrai's CO2 emission will be
0.97 kg per kWh, a more than 20% reduction over 2005 figures.


Page 136 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Matrai Chairman Jozsef Valaska noted that the free distribution of CO2 emission quotas will end as of
2013, meaning that the company will have to buy quotas at auction, resulting in significantly higher quota
spending than that of more efficient gas-fired power plants. While this concern prompted Matrai to
suspend plans to build the new block as recently as January this year, the jump in gas prices has made
the company confident that the new block will be profitable.

"If we have to buy quotas after 2013, we will have to pay 30% more [than gas-fired power plants], but the price of lignite is only one-fourth that of natural gas," Valaska said.

The chairman added that while gas is almost exclusively imported, lignite (or brown coal) is mined locally, with reserves expected to last for at least another 120 years.

Valaska said the company also expects to eventually install equipment that could subtract CO2 from the power plant's exhaust, which would eliminate the need for quotas.

According to the company's calculations, the new block will help preserve Matrai's position as Hungary's second cheapest electricity source after the Paks nuclear power plant, a unit of MVM. The HUF 200-bln investment will be recovered in a period of about 25 years, Valaska added.

MVM CEO Martha said the project will be 20% financed from equity and 80% from a syndicated loan. Martha added that the ongoing global financial crisis is not expected to have a serious impact on the company's plans.

"By the time we actually get to take out these loans, the crisis will likely have calmed down," he said. "Besides, the energy sector is still among the most profitable for banks to finance."

not adequately encourage the production of energy from renewable sources either.

The news site noted that the German bank also plans to start energy trading in Romania,= once it receives all necessary permits.

WARSAW. SEPTEMBER 29. INTERFAX CENTRAL  EUROPE - Hungarian OTP Bank is mulling a debut on the Warsaw Stock Exchange by the end of 2008, OTP Chairman and CEO Sandor Csanyi told reporters Monday.

"I hope that we may debut on the Warsaw Stock Exchange by the end of this year," Csanyi said. "In this case it will be a dual listing with Hungary."

OTP plans to launch a road show in October to promote the bank to Polish investors, which currently constitute the bank's second largest investor group. It will not be issuing any new shares as part of the debut and has a 2009 target to join Warsaw's blue-chip index WIG20.

Polish media have speculated on the possibility of a merger between OTP and Poland's No. 2 bank in terms of assets, PKO BP. Csanyi admitted that the banks have negotiated some form of partnership but did not want to disclose details.

"I can't go into detail but there has been work and we looked for cooperation between the two banks but we never reached the idea of a merger," Csanyi said. "We are interested in certain cooperation with PKO BP but I can't give any details for the time being."

While OTP recognizes the need for caution during a turbulent time on financial markets, according to Csanyi, the bank has already survived the major crisis, which hit Russian and Asian markets in 1992, and expects to survive this one.

"There is no financial institution that is not worried about the financial market, but we can influence this situation [...]," Csanyi told Interfax. "The Warsaw Stock Exchange valuation given to institutions in Poland is higher than the same comparative basis given to Hungarian institutions and it is important to be listed on more liquid markets."
Page 137 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
KYIV. Oct 3 (Interfax) - Naftogaz Ukrainy and Russia's gas monopolist Gazprom will in the next few
weeks sign long-term contracts on the delivery of natural gas to and its transit through Ukraine, Ukrainian
Prime Minister Yulia Tymoshenko told a news conference on Friday.

"We instructed economic agents – Naftogaz Ukrainy and Gazprom – to sign contracts on the delivery and transit of natural gas in the next few weeks with regard to an intergovernmental memorandum signed on Thursday," Tymoshenko said.
The contracts will be signed for at least ten years, the premier said.

Ukraine and Russia agreed on the gradual switch to  European pricing for gas and its transit services, she added.

Gas prices for Ukraine will grow together with the growth of tariffs for the transit of Russian gas through the country, Tymoshenko said.

The Ukrainian prime minister did not disclose the expected gas price for Ukraine in 2009, noting that it will become clearer after Gazprom agrees on the terms of buying gas from Central Asian producers.

YEREVAN. Oct 3 (Interfax) - Russian Foreign Minister Sergei Lavrov thinks that artificial NATO eastward expansion, which can be explained by the mindset of exploring new territories, is dangerous to Russia, Russian Foreign Minister Sergei Lavrov said.

"The threat is in artificial NATO eastward expansion, which takes place as a part of the mindset of exploring new territories and which is not justified and does not correspond with any real threat," Lavrov told a news conference in Yerevan in the wake of talks with Armenian Foreign Minister Edward Nalbandian.

Russia, however, sees no threat if NATO evolves in an alliance that counters real challenges and threats, the minister said.
Russia and Armenia see eye-to-eye on what kind of NATO they would like to cooperate with, he said. Russia does have interaction with NATO, he said. In particular, both Russia and Armenia are members of
the Euroatlantic Partnership Council, Lavrov said.
ST. PETERSBURG. Oct 2 (Interfax) - External factors have caused "minimal" damage to
Russian-German relations, Russian President Dmitry Medvedev said on Thursday.

"Our premise is that such effects [of external factors] on our relations have at the end of the day done minimal damage to our relations. What explains this is not only the interdependence of our economies, their mutual influence, and public openness to dialogue," Medvedev said at Russian-German talks in St. Petersburg.

Such a cooperative attitude characterizes the position of those who "make ultimate decisions [and] shape international policy both in Moscow and in Berlin," Medvedev said.

There are points on which Russia and Germany are seriously divided, the president said. "But we try - and our German partners do the same - to understand each other, to work for our shared future. And by and large we succeed," he said.

Germany and Russia disagree on some issues of foreign and security policy and that this primarily applies to the recent conflict in Georgia, German Chancellor Angela Merkel said.

However, she credited Russia and Germany with maintaining stable and direct contacts throughout the conflict.
Germany had contributed to the plan for the settlement of the Georgian conflict, she also said. Merkel expressed optimism about future German-Russian relations.
ST. PETERSBURG. Oct 2 (Interfax) – Countries that do not participate in the Nord Stream gas pipeline project should not hinder its development, Russian President Dmitry Medvedev said on Thursday during the Russian-German top-level interstate consultations.

"We are hoping that countries that are not related to this project will not try to impact its development," Medvedev said.



Page 138 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"The profit from this project is obvious. We believe it will be good for all of its participants. In any case, we are hoping for progress within the time that we have planned," he added.

The 1,200-km Nord Stream gas pipeline is supposed to link Russia's Baltic coast near Vyborg with Germany's Baltic coast near Greifswald. In the initial phase, Russia should supply 27.5 billion cubic meters of gas per year to Germany via the pipeline. Capacity is to be increased to 55 billion cubic meters per year after a second strand of the pipeline has been built.

Russian gas monopolist Gazprom owns 51% in the project, Germany's E.ON and BASF have 20% each, and the Dutch Gasunie owns 9%.

The Nord Stream project, circumventing Poland as a transit country for gas, has been criticized by most Polish political parties as too expensive compared to a land alternative. The Baltic States and Sweden opposed the Nord Stream project on ecological grounds.

Polish governments have in the past also expressed concern that with Nord Stream Russia may use gas supplies as a political weapon, reducing gas supplies to Central  Europe without affecting deliveries to Germany.

MOSCOW. Sept 30 (Interfax) - The Main Military Prosecutor's Office has granted the relatives of the Polish officers shot in Katyn in 1943 access to classified documents, including the decree to close the criminal case on the basis of the military men's killings. Access to the documents was granted under a court ruling.

On Tuesday, the Moscow District Military Court proposed to consider a complaint filed by the relatives of the Polish officers about the decision to stop the investigation into the officers' killings, the plaintiffs' lawyer Anna Stavitskaya told Interfax.

"In the past hearing, we requested access to the decree to stop the criminal case and the case materials about the people we represent. The court granted that request and the Main Military Prosecutor's Office provided the documents," she said.

"We have been asked to give our written undertaking not to disclose information constituting a state secret as the decree and some of the materials are classified information," she said.

Stavitskaya also said the court has adjourned until October 9 to enable the plaintiffs to become familiar with the case materials.
The case is being tried in the Moscow City Court without journalists present.

The Moscow Khamovnichesky District Court earlier refused to consider the complaint about the closure of the investigation into the so-called Katyn case. The Moscow City Court on July 7 let that ruling stand.

Commenting on the ruling issued by the Khamovnichesky District Court, Stavitskaya said the judge made that decision citing the presence of classified information in the case materials.
"The court did not consider our complaint on its merits," she said,

During the trial, the court received a message from the Prosecutor General's Office, which stated that the Katyn case materials constitute classified information as they contain information constituting a state secret. The Prosecutor General's Office said in its message that the materials can be provided to the Moscow District Military Court, where the complaint is to be tried.

Earlier, officials from the Main Military Prosecutor's Office also pointed out that the case materials contained classified information, saying the case could not be tried by a district court for that reason,
The decree to close the Katyn case was issued by the Main Military Prosecutor's office on September 21,
2004. The victims' relatives contested that ruling.

MOSCOW. Sept 30 (Interfax-AVN) - President Dmitry Medvedev has urged the heads of the Russian military to strengthen the strategic nuclear deterrence forces and to create an integrated system of
air-space defense.

"Concurrently with the army and navy's upgrade and efforts to make them more mobile, the strategic nuclear deterrence forces must be strengthened, a single air-space defense system must be created and Russia's status as a great naval power must be enhanced," Medvedev told officers just appointed to high command posts in the Grand Kremlin Palace on Tuesday.


Page 139 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Also, the armed forces' control bodies and "the state's entire military organization, must be perfected," Medvedev said.
Recent times have set new tasks before the country's law enforcement system, the president said.

"A swift identification of external threats to national security by the Russian foreign intelligence service acquires special importance," he said.
MOSCOW. Sept 29 (Interfax-AVN) - The proposed U.S. missile shield is apparently targeted against
Russia, former Russian General Staff Chief Yury Baluyevsky said.
"The anti-Russian potential of this system will increase, and no one should be tricked into thinking that
the missile shield is being created to control Iran and North Korea. Where is Eastern  Europe? And where
is Pyongyang, or Tehran?" Baluyevsky said in an interview, published by the Versia newspaper on
Monday.

If the United States had been indeed concerned about the Iranian missile-dangerous direction while deploying a radar installation in the Czech Republic, it would have chosen a different site, such as the Gabala radar in Azerbaijan, rented by Russia, he said.

"Would it not have been easier to accept our proposal and make use of the tracking facility already in place in Azerbaijan?" Baluyevsky asked in a rhetorical question.
Instead, the United States is "re-inventing the wheel," Baluyevsky said.

"We [Russia] are the issue here. [The U.S. missile shield] as well as control over the Russian armed forces, and - we cannot discard this, either - preparing a strike against our country. Let it be a supposition for now. Let it be something not talked too much about, but the U.S. does see our country as its potential enemy," Baluyevsky said.

In this context, Russia should observe a balance and "not spend more than it can afford on arms, but not try to economize on its security, either," he said.

TBILISI. Sept 29 (Interfax) - Georgian Ambassador to Russia Erosi Kitsmarishvili has announced that it is impossible for him to continue working for the administration of President Mikheil Saakashvili.

"I cannot work for the Saakashvili team because they failed to prevent a war with Russia," Kitsmarishvili said in a interview with the Georgian weekly Kviris Palitra on Monday. The ambassador was withdrawn from Moscow on July 11, 2008.
In his opinion, the Georgian administration could have avoided hostilities with Russia.

"If the Georgian administration had spent [on the prevention of war] at least one percent of funds assigned for military purposes, I can assure you that the result would have exceeded the widest expectations," Kitsmarishvili said.
Western leaders and diplomats kept warning Saakashvili against attempts to resolve the conflicts in
Abkhazia and South Ossetia by force, he said.

"One high-ranking Western diplomat wrote that each warning had an effect for only two weeks and then military rhetoric resumed," he said.

If late Prime Minister Zurab Zhvania had been alive, the events in Georgia would have taken a peaceful course, the diplomat said.

"The death of Zhvania was not natural; it was a murder. At the same time, I cannot say who benefited from Zhvania's death," Kitsmarishvili said.

HD        EU fines Hungarian  oil, gas company  over reported  cartel
WC       350 words
PD        5 October 2008
ET        03:08 PM
SN        BBC Monitoring European
SC        BBCEUP
LA        English
CY        (c) 2008 The British Broadcasting Corporation. All Rights Reserved. No material may be reproduced except with the express permission of The British Broadcasting Corporation.

LP
Text of report by Hungarian privately-owned conservative newspaper Magyar Nemzet, on 2 October
[Unattributed report: "MOL Punished for Operating  Cartel"]

TD
The European Commission has imposed a total  fine of 676 million euros on nine oil companies, including MOL [Hungarian Oil and Gas] for operating a cartel. MOL responded by announcing that the company would be giving due consideration to the Commission's decision and would clarify its position in two months on whether it would appeal to the European court of first instance in Luxembourg. The Commission fined MOL 23.7 million  euros.

Paraffin wax is used in a wide variety of products such as candles,  waxed paper, tires, and car parts. The market is worth nearly 500 million  euros, according to the Hungarian MTI news agency. MOL released a statement, saying: MOL regrets that in the 1990s it was not possible for the company to have adequate internal control and auditing systems to screen such cases. It added that there had never
been a management decision to take part in any kind of  cartel.

Neelie Kroes, European commissioner for competition, announced yesterday that the companies in the cartel fixed prices for paraffin  wax and thus distorted competition. The highest  fine, 318 million euros, was imposed on the German-South African Sasol; the other companies, ENI, Exxon-Mobil, Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol, Repsol, RWE, and Total were  fined sums between 12 and 128 million euros. According to the  European Commission, those companies participated in a  cartel for paraffin  wax between 1992 and 2005 in violation of the Commission's treaty ban on cartels and restrictive business practices. Shell, which was also part of the cartel, blew the operation's cover and escaped  fines after supplying information on the  cartel's activities to the European Commission. Sasol's  fine was increased by 50 per cent because Sasol led the cartel. ENI's fine was increased by 60 per cent, as the company had taken part in similar cartels before.
Source: Magyar Nemzet, Budapest, in Hungarian 2 Oct 08; p 12

SE     Week in brief
HD     EC fines "paraffin mafia"  cartel €676m
WC    1,413 words
PD     6 October 2008
SN     ICIS Chemical Business
SC     ACHEN
VOL   361
LA     English
CY     Copyright 2008. Reed Business Information Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
EC fines "paraffin  mafia" cartel €676m

The European Commission has fined nine petrochemical companies a total of €676m ($950m) for taking part in an industry-wide paraffin  wax trading cartel. Italy's ENI, US-based energy and chemical giant ExxonMobil, German oils firms Hansen & Rosenthal and Tudapetrol, Hungary's MOL, Spain's oil and gas firm Repsol, South Africa's Sasol, German power firm RWE, and French petrochemical giant Total were all found guilty of  price fixing  between 1992 and 2005. Sasol's  fine was increased by 50% for leading the cartel, while ENI was ordered to pay 60% more for previous activities in similar cartels. The Commission said that in the Shell group, the  cartel was called "paraffin  mafia," and in the Sasol group, "Blauer Salon" (blue saloon), after a hotel bar in Germany where the first meetings of the  cartel took
place.

TD
wax Producers claim innocence

Paraffin wax producers said they were in the dark on the exact reasons why they have been  fined by the European Commission for price fixing,  with some considering court appeals. Germany's Hansen & Rosenthal, which was fined a total of €36m ($51m), said it would look to appeal and that it "continues to rule the fine as unjustified." South Africa-based Sasol, which received the largest fine of €318m, said it
was surprised by, and "does not understand the reasons for the magnitude of this fine and will be studying the reasons for the finding with a view to lodge an appeal against it."
Delaware court rules in favor of Huntsman

A Delaware, US, state court has ruled in favor of US-based chemical firm Huntsman by ordering a speedy completion of its merger with US-based rival Hexion Specialty Chemicals. "We are gratified that Apollo's [Hexion's parent company] allegations and tactics have failed to persuade the Chancery Court," said Peter Huntsman, president and CEO of Huntsman. "We are disappointed by the Court's decision. We are reviewing the decision and our options," Hexion said in a statement.
Huntsman sues banks

US-based chemical firm Huntsman is suing the two banks that are financing its $10.6bn (€7.3bn) merger with Hexion Specialty Chemicals. In the suit, Huntsman accused global bank Credit Suisse and Germany's Deutsche Bank of conspiring with Hexion's parent firm - Apollo Management - to interfere with the merger. Huntsman has received a temporary restraining order against the banks, which stops them from ending their commitments to finance the merger with Hexion.
AkzoNobel ups synergy

Netherlands-based specialties firm AkzoNobel is planning a raft of measures to achieve additional cost savings and improve performance. Leif Darner, AkzoNobel board member and head of performance coatings, said that part of the company's plans included accelerating efforts to integrate the UK-based ICI businesses acquired earlier this year as well as focusing on organic growth. In addition to the €340m ($490m) saved through the ICI integration, AkzoNobel will aim for additional net savings of €100m. The combined effect of this will be around 3,500 job losses by 2011.
Pressure groups attack BMS expansion


Page 146 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
German pressure groups have challenged chemical company Bayer MaterialScience's plans for a €300m ($423m) upgrade of isocyanates production in Germany. The Coordination Against Bayer Dangers, and the German Friends of the Earth Affiliate, BUND, called on BMS not to proceed with the consolidation and expansion of production at Brunsbuttel and Dormagen/Uerdingen up to 2013. They are also protesting against the use of phosgene-based technology in the new projects.
SAFC doubles output at arklow unit
Changing attitudes among major pharmaceutical companies toward quality and cost issues have played a key role in doubling the output of US-based SAFC Pharma's manufacturing facility in Arklow, Ireland,
since its acquisition from US industrial supplies manufacturer Honeywell two years ago, said Mike Harris, vice president of sales and manufacturing at SAFC Pharma. "We are seeing with many of the compounds that Arklow are making a real resurgence in volume," he said at the CPhI '08 trade show in Frankfurt, Germany.
German industries unite against ETS

Germany's energy-intensive industrial producers warned of a €7.2bn ($10.1bn) cost burden by 2020 if the European Union's Emission Trading Scheme (ETS) goes into law as is proposed. "If the  European          Commission's ETS proposal passes without changes, the deindustrialization of Germany will be inevitable," said Ulrich Lehner, president of Germany's Chemical Industry Association (VCI).
Brenntag buys Rhodia's Asian arm

German chemical distributor Brenntag has purchased Rhodia's Asia-Pacific specialty chemical distribution business. The deal gives Brenntag access to fast-growing distribution markets in Australia, India,
Thailand, Indonesia, the Philippines, Singapore, and Vietnam, the company said at the  European Petrochemical Association (EPCA) meeting in Monaco. Rhodia's Asia-Pacific distribution network achieved total sales of $400m (€290m) in 2007.
Sumitomo halves h1 earnings estimate

Japan's Sumitomo Chemical has halved its forecast for consolidated net income for the first half of the year to yen (Y) 5bn ($47.5m) due to the worsening climate in the petrochemical and basic chemical segments. For the full year ending March 2009, Sumitomo expects net income to be Y15bn, down by
72.7% from the previously forecast Y55bn.
Holliday to get Palladium Medal

The Societe de Chimie Indus-trielle - American Section, announced that the 2009 International Palladium Medal will be awarded to Chad Holliday, chairman and CEO of US-based chemical giant DuPont. The award is in recognition of Holliday's contributions to the industry, and thereby to the enhancement of the international aims and objectives of the trade organization. He will receive the medal at a dinner in his honor on May 5.
LANXESS starts up new EPDM plant

Germany's LANXESS has started production at a new pelletizing plant for ethylene-propylene-diene monomer (EPDM) rubber in Marl, Germany. The new facility has capacity to produce 3-5 tonnes/hour of pellets, opening up new market opportunities in the cable industry and for thermoplastic olefins.
Yansab delays Saudi MEG start-up to 2009

The start-up of monoethylene glycol (MEG) operations at Yanbu National Petrochemical (Yansab) will be delayed to early 2009 from the fourth quarter of 2008, said a source from parent company SABIC. The
700,000 tonne/year MEG plant is located at Yanbu in Saudi Arabia.
Sharq MEG delayed

Saudi Arabia-based Eastern Petrochemical's (Sharq) No. 4 monoethylene glycol (MEG) line in Al-Jubail, with 700,000 tonnes/year of capacity, will be delayed beyond October, but will start up before the end of the year, said a source at Saudi chemical giant SABIC. Sharq is a joint venture of Japanese companies, led by Mitsubishi Chemical, and SABIC.
Fortis woes could hurt petchem trading


Page 147 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The near-collapse in  European bank and insurer Fortis could squeeze credit lines for petrochemical traders. Two sources from an international trading house said at EPCA that they had used Fortis for credit on a variety of projects and that they were not alone. "Fortis are lenders big time. Everyone has business with them," said one trader. Fortis's financial problems "are really slowing things down."
cefic aims for 100% plastics recycling

The EU, US and Japan should be recycling 100% of plastics waste by 2030,  European Chemical Industry Council (Cefic) president Francois Cornelis said. The global production of plastics is set to double to 350m tonnes/year by 2030, and the issue of disposal should be placed high on the agenda, said Cornelis, also president of chemicals at French oil and chemical giant Total. "I can't understand how in  Europe we still put plastics into landfills. I think burning plastics instead of coal should be easy and we need to get to the solution much faster. Why can't we substitute 10% of the coal burned in power plants for plastics" he said.
Azelis preregisters

European distributor Azelis has revealed that it was one of two companies to have preregistered all
100,000 chemicals listed in the EU inventory with the  European Chemicals Agency for Reach.

SE            Business Report
HD            Sasol’s BEE investors are learning  the hardest way
BY            James Chikwanha
WC           567 words
PD            6 October 2008
SN            The Star SC            THESTR ED            e1
PG            14
LA             English
CY            © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
But life has many twist and turns, and a lot has happened over the few intervening weeks.

The crude oil price has fallen sharply, with the benchmark Brent blend dropping from about $115 a barrel at the start of September to about $94 on Friday. It could fall further, as the global economy slows and demand for oil subsides.

TD
So Sasol shareholders’ returns look less promising than they did a month ago.
But much worse is the news that Sasol’s  European wax division has been brought to book by the
European Commission for anti-competitive behaviour.

And, as part of what the commission described as the “paraffin  mafia” cartel, the multinational petrochemical company has been fined E318.2 million (R3.8 billion) – the fourth-largest  fine the commission has ever imposed.

The penalty, which is nearly 10 times Sasol  Wax’s R381 million operating profit in the year to June, must be paid over the next few months.
And Sasol, which has known about the investigation for some time, made no provision for the  fine
because, it said, it had not known how much it would be.
Now it will set aside R3.8 billion, which will make a serious dent in Sasol’s profit in the current
financial year. Sasol says it will pay the  fine but it plans to appeal against the  commission’s findings.

Meanwhile, the new shareholders are already deep in negative territory, with the share trading 2 percent lower at R305 on Friday. It later closed at R308.

As many of the BEE shareholders have been heavily funded by third parties – perhaps as many as 90 percent says one analyst – the fall in the share price and the reduced dividend streams will have further consequences.

The episode is an example of the booby traps life sets for the unwary. But even the wary could have fallen into this trap.
Only someone with a crystal ball could have foreseen the events that unfolded over this month. There is an inherent problem in trying to accumulate wealth and manage savings, particularly in the
current uncertain environment. Investing in equities puts capital at risk.

But investing in bonds or putting money in a bank exposes the capital to the risk of erosion by inflation. Moreover, after a certain threshold, investors have to pay tax on their interest rate returns.



Page 149 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The worst case scenario for bank savings is that the bank could collapse. This has become a common occurrence in the US, while the UK and  Europe have also seen banks fold this year.

No South African bank is at risk at the moment, but Saambou was forced into curatorship in 2002, when depositors withdrew about R1 billion over two days.

Perhaps the only lesson to be learnt from the episode is the obvious one: that high debt levels are dangerous because the unexpected often happens.

Many consumers and householders have learnt this lesson over the past few years, as debt service costs rose. And more will be exposed to the consequences of taking on too much debt, as falling asset prices create a negative equity trap.
In the current environment, cans of beans may be the best form of investment.

The alternative is to spend your surpluses on mood-altering substances and leave the world to take care of itself.

The European Commission slapped fines worth a total of EUR 676 M on nine petrochemical companies for their involvement in an industry-wide paraffin  wax trading cartel. Those fined were the USA's ExxonMobil, Italy's ENI, South African firm Sasol, German firm Hansen & Rosenthal, Tudapetrol and

EUROPEAN UNION -- The  European Commission has fined nine companies, including Exxon Mobil, Total and South Africa's Sasol, almost $1 billion for operating a  price-fixing "paraffin  mafia." The nine were found guilty of participating in a  paraffin wax cartel from 1992 to 2005 in the European Economic Area in violation of EU rules. A tenth company, Royal Dutch Shell, had its fine waived after its tip-off led to surprise raids by the commission in April 2005. The steepest, $445 million fine was levied on Sasol, which the commission identified as the ringleader, while Total was  fined $179 million and Exxon $117 million. Others fined included Germany's RWE, Italy's Eni, Hungary's Mol and Spain's Repsol YPF.

TD
Sasol's  fine would have been double had it not cooperated, while Eni's  fine was increased by 60%
because of its involvement in other cartels dating back to the 1980s.

HD            Another  round of price-fixing fines EC hits nine wax makers with $930 million in penalties
BY             European Rubber Journal
WC           559 words
PD            6 October 2008
SN            Rubber & Plastics News
SC            RBPN
PG            0004
VOL          Volume 38; Number 5
LA             English
CY            (c) 2008 Crain Communications, Inc. All rights reserved.

LP
The European Commission has imposed a total of more than $930 million in  fines on nine companies for participating in a  cartel for paraffin wax.

A 10th company—Royal Dutch Shell P.L.C.—participated in the  cartel but was not  fined because it blew the whistle on the  cartel, according to the  commission. Three other companies had their  fines reduced because they cooperated with the investigation.

TD
Paraffin waxes are used in a wide variety of products, including tires and car components as well as in the rubber, packaging, adhesives and chewing gum industries. The market is worth almost $700 million.
The companies and the amounts  fined were:

* Sasol Ltd. of South Africa and Germany, $440 million. It received the largest  fine, which was reduced 50 percent from its original total because of its cooperation.
* Repsol S.A. of Spain, $27.3 million. Its  fine was reduced by 25 percent for cooperation.
* ExxonMobil Corp. of the U.S., $115.4 million. It received a 7-percent reduction on its original  fine.
* Eni S.p.A. of Italy, $40.2 million.
* Tudapetrol of Germany, $16.6 million.
* Hansen & Rosenthal of Germany, $33.1 million.
* MOL Nyrt of Hungary, $32.7 million.
* RWE A.G. of Germany, $51.7 million.
* Total S.A. of France, $176.8 million.

The infringements committed by ExxonMobil, Sasol, Shell, RWE and Total also related to slack  wax sold to end-customers on the German market. Slack  wax is the raw material required for the manufacture of  paraffin waxes and is produced in refineries as a by-product in the manufacture of base oils from crude oil.

"There is probably not a household or company in  Europe that has not bought products affected by this `paraffin  mafia'  cartel, with all that implies in terms of paying over the odds, higher costs and economic damage," said Competition Commissioner Neelie Kroes. "Such illegal  cartel behavior cannot and will not be tolerated by the  commission, and companies' managers and shareholders should take note."

The EC probe started with surprise inspections in April 2005, prompted by an application from Shell for immunity under the 2002 Leniency Notice.

Page 156 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The commission said that from 1992 to 2005 the producers of  paraffin waxes and slack wax operated a cartel in which they fixed prices for  paraffin waxes. ExxonMobil, MOL, Repsol, Sasol, Shell and Total also engaged in market allocation for this product, the EC statement said.

The companies held regular meetings to discuss prices, allocate markets and/or customers, and to exchange sensitive commercial information, according to the EC. The  commission said that within the Shell group, the  cartel was called "paraffin  mafia" and in the Sasol group, "Blauer Salon"—or blue saloon—after a hotel bar in Germany where the first meetings of the  cartel took place. Subsequent meetings took place at a series of hotels all over  Europe, including Milan, Italy; Vienna, Austria; Budapest, Hungary; Paris; Munich, Germany; and Strasbourg, France.
Oil supplier Hansen & Rosenthal said it intends to fight its  fine. It said companies within its group
were fined a total of nearly $50 million, but it hasn't received written substantiation for all of the  fines.
The company said that its board "sees grounds for substantial legal objections."

Dutch Shell PLC | rwe : RWE AG | ssol : Sasol Ltd

Nine firms have been  fined a total of EUR 676 M ($974 M) by the European Commission for colluding to fix paraffin wax prices in 1992-2005 in Europe. The heaviest fines were imposed on Sasol at EUR 318.2
M, Total at EUR 128.2 M, and ExxonMobil at EUR 83.6 M. The other firms that were  fined are Italy's Eni, Germany's Hansen & Rosenthal, Hungary's MOL, Repsol, RWE, and Tudapetrol. Another member of the cartel, Shell, was able to escape its EUR 96 M  fine because it informed the  commission of the existence of the cartel.

SE            EUROPE, MIDDLE EAST & AFRICA
HD           South Africa's Sasol to appeal EU fine of $455 million for operating wax cartel
BY           Jacinta Moran
WC          315 words
PD           6 October 2008
SN           Platts Oilgram News
SC           PON
PG           4
VOL        ISSN: 0163-1284, Volume 86, Issue 197
LA           English
CY           (c) 2008 McGraw-Hill, Inc.

LP
South Africa's Sasol plans to appeal the  European Commission's decision to  fine one of its subsidiaries and a group of refiners including ExxonMobil, Total and Eni a total of Eur676 million ($969.5 million) for operating a  paraffin wax price-fixing cartel, Sasol CEO Pat Davies said October
3.

"While a  fine was expected, we didn't anticipate the magnitude of the  fine, particularly in the light of, amongst other factors, not being the legacy owner," Davies said. "We do not yet have the detailed reasoning from the EC and will study it, as soon as it is supplied, to clarify our next steps. As we see things now, it is our intention to appeal."

TD
The European Commission said October 1 that from 1992 to 2005 the producers of  paraffin wax
and its feedstock slack wax in the European Economic Area operated a "paraffin  mafia" in which they
fixed prices for paraffin  waxes (ON 10/2).

The EC gave Sasol a  fine of Eur318.2 million ($455.12 million), the largest for an individual company, because of its role as leader of the  cartel, the EC said.

"We regret that this has occurred in one of Sasol's subsidiaries and that our due diligence and compliance programs failed to identify this anti-competitive behavior, when we acquired the business
13 years ago and thereafter," Davies said.

He said Sasol's management, including the Sasol directors of the Schumann-Sasol joint venture, was totally unaware of these activities. "When we became aware of them in 2005, they were immediately stopped and Sasol promptly began cooperating with the  European Commission's investigation," he said.

These cartel activities were "clearly reprehensible" and constituted a flagrant breach of Sasol's policies and values, Davies added.

RE           safr : South Africa | africaz : Africa | devgcoz : Emerging Market Countries | dvpcoz : Developing
Economies | souafrz : Southern Africa

SE           Business Report
HD          Sasol ‘totally unaware’ of cartel
BY           paul.smith
WC         596 words
PD          6 October 2008
SN          The Star SC          THESTR ED          e1
PG          15
LA           English
CY          © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
The European wax division of Sasol, bust last week by the  European Commission’s competition watchdog for leading a price rigging “paraffin  mafia”, ironically began making significant profit only after the  cartel was exposed.
“Our view is this was an ineffective  cartel,” Sasol chief executive Pat Davies said on his return to
South Africa on Friday from an investor road show in the US.

TD
Davies immediately held a media briefing to deal with the fallout of the e381 million (R4.5 billion)
penalty slapped on the petrochemicals producer for its role in the  paraffin waxes cartel.
Other members included ExxonMobil, Shell and Total.

“This was a business that hardly made any money until a couple of years ago,” Davies said of the European wax unit. “Ironically, it started making significant money after 2005. This is a cyclical business so it’s had its moments, but generally it’s not been a particularly profitable business.”

Davies emphasised that while Sasol accepted responsibility for the actions of the unit, the company had been “totally unaware” of the  cartel’s activities until the  commission launched its investigation in April 2005.

He said the first meetings of the  cartel took place three years before Sasol took a stake in the division and a decade before the company bought full control of the unit.

Sasol had been taken by surprise by the magnitude of the  fine, particularly because it was not the original owner of the unit, he said.
“As we see things now, it is our intention to appeal.”

The company would await detailed reasoning from the commission before deciding the basis of the appeal, but one aspect was likely to be the size of the fine, he said.

Sasol’s penalty comprises nearly half of the total  fine imposed by the commission on the 10 cartel members. Although the  fine was reduced by 50 percent because Sasol co-operated with the commission, it was also raised by 50 percent because the company was named as the leader of the cartel.

Davies firmly denied that Sasol was the leader, although he conceded it was the largest operator in the market.

There was no evidence that the individuals involved in the  cartel, who had all since left Sasol, had gained personally from their involvement. Davies suspected their motive had been to enhance what they considered to be the success of the business.


Page 160 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
In a sign of how strongly cash generative Sasol is in a climate of generally high oil prices, the company is to draw on internal cash resources to pay the  fine – which must be deposited with the  commission in three months, regardless of whether an appeal is lodged. Future projects will not be affected.

Sasol had “substantial strength” in its balance sheet to fund projects and remain within its self-imposed gearing ratio of between 30 percent and 50 percent, Davies said. At the end of the 2008 financial year in June, Sasol’s debt-to-equity ratio was 20 percent.

Davies said that Sasol had not previously provided for the fine because it had been impossible to determine how much provision to make because of the commission’s wide discretion.

The EU’s tough regulatory framework on competition issues meant that Sasol would in future be “extremely careful” with its due diligence and compliance procedures, were it to make any acquisitions in the region in future, Davies said.

The company had recently strengthened its compliance programmes, with a particular focus on high-risk areas such as joint ventures.

SE          Business Report
HD         Amended antitrust bill eases up on directors
BY          Donwald Pressly
WC        671 words
PD         8 October 2008
SN         The Star SC         THESTR ED         e1
PG         18
LA          English
CY         © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
A national assembly committee has approved amendments to the Competition Amendment Bill to relax a clause that would have set a stringent negligence standard for criminal sanctions on directors, even if they were unaware of  cartel arrangements.

The legislation was brought back to the trade and industry portfolio committee after amendments were made by the national council of provinces (NCOP). The original bill passed through the assembly committee, chaired by ANC MP Ben Martins, and the assembly itself in August.

TD
A cartel can be defined as a group of businesses that aims to fix prices and limit supply and competition. Such conduct could include  price-fixing, collusive tendering, bid rigging, market carving and excluding competition.

Zodwa Ntuli, the deputy director-general at the department of trade and industry, said yesterday that after discussion with stakeholders and consideration of points raised in the select committee in the NCOP, the department had realised that there “may be an issue of fairness” in the case of directors who were not aware of  cartel arrangements.

There were some arguments that the stringent provision was unconstitutional, but the department had redrafted the relevant clause in consideration of the fairness argument.

Asked for an example, Ntuli said that four competitors might come together and make a  price-fixing arrangement. A director of one of the firms – represented at the meeting – was not aware of the arrangement and was not present. “In that case, it would be a bit unfair to say that director, who had no knowledge of that conduct, could be criminally liable.”

Asked how the bill now differed from the Competition Act of 1998, she said that previously only companies were liable for  cartel behaviour and not individual directors. Now, directors who were aware of the cartel behaviour would be liable for prosecution alongside their companies. Only those who were not aware would fall outside this sanction.
In addition, for the first time, individual directors would be responsible for the payment of the sanctions. In terms of the legislation, a contravention would carry a  fine “not exceeding R500 000” or
imprisonment for a period not exceeding 10 years, or both.

Under the current act, such a penalty is reserved for individuals who contravened an order of the competition tribunal or the competition appeal court. In the case of other offences, the  fine was limited to R2 000, imprisonment for six months or both.

Ntuli noted that assessment of whether a director – or even a member of staff of a company – was aware of cartel behaviour could arise following application by leniency applicants or whistle-blowers, or through a search and seizure operation by the competition  commission.

Page 180 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
According to the website Polity.org, competition tribunal chairman David Lewis added his voice to concern that the provision regarding personal liability for directors could be found to be in conflict with the constitution.

At that point, the bill said a director would have committed an offence if he or she was responsible for causing the firm to engage in a prohibited practice, or knowingly acquiesced by either having actual knowledge of the behaviour or occupying a position in which he or she reasonably should have been aware.
While the amendments allow for directors to be criminally liable, they must know of the  cartel
behaviour.

The current legislation allows for a maximum penalty of 10 percent of total turnover of a company found to have engaged in  cartel behaviour.

A recent example of  cartel behaviour is a R145 million settlement between the competition commission and New Reclamation Group over its involvement in collusion and  price-fixing in the scrap metal market. Last year the  commission fined Tiger Brands nearly R100 million for bread overpricing. Tiger Brands acknowledged that it had colluded with other milling companies.
Sasol has recently been slapped with a fine of e318 million (R4.3 billion at yesterday’s exchange rate)
for alleged fixing of  paraffin wax prices in Europe between 1992 and 2005.

SE           News
HD          How will Sasol be held to account  in South Africa for its misdeeds  in Europe? BY           Alide Dasnois
WC          863 words
PD           9 October 2008
SN           Cape Times
SC           CAPTIM
ED           e1
PG          9
LA           English
CY           © Copyright 2008 Independent Newspapers (UK) Limited. All rights reserved.

LP
If there was a week to “bury bad news”, then last week certainly was one of them. With enough domestic political turmoil, it was anything but a slow news week. No one would have been more relieved than Sasol chief executive Pat Davies
Synfuel producer Sasol was  fined a whopping R3.7 billion by the  European Commission for
price-fixing and leading the so-called “paraffin wax mafia” last week.

TD
Sasol, the commission found, was the leader of the nine-company  cartel which dominated the
paraffin  wax market in Europe for 13 years.

Essentially, these large companies made the decision to fix prices in the lucrative  paraffin wax industry. The  European Commission has been investigating the matter for a number of years, after, interestingly enough, a company itself involved in the  price-fixing affair blew the whistle on Sasol and the other companies in the  cartel.
Sasol received by far the highest  fine for its illegal activities.

The finding by the  commission is damning. It exposes the price-fixers as having full knowledge that what they were doing was illegal. Sasol has sufficient cash reserves and so appears comfortable that it is in a position to pay the  fine within the required three months.
As has been pointed out elsewhere, it was not Sasol’s first brush with anti-competitive behaviour. In
2005, the Competition  Commission in South Africa  fined the company for fixing fertiliser prices. Spot
the theme.
The blatantly illegal behaviour again raises the fundamental question of corporate accountability.

As Neelie Kroes of the  European Commission has warned, there is a greater need for shareholder activism and management accountability to prevent some of these excesses.

While Sasol might appeal the ruling, it is its reputation which remains at risk. It has in its PR tried to drive home the fact that the transformation of the company is under way and that it wants to reflect certain “values”.
Which values, one must ask?

Serious questions have to be asked of companies; where are the accountability deficits in the way in which companies are managed and in the oversight which boards have?

In September last year, Mittal Steel was  fined R700 million by the Competition Tribunal for “excessive” pricing in the steel industry. At the time, Trade and Industry Minister Mandisi Mpahlwa talked of giving the Competition  Commission greater powers to investigate uncompetitive behaviour pro-actively.


Page 182 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Regulation is important, as is an effective body to investigate and follow-up on issues of  price-fixing
or any anti-competitive behaviour.

But, more importantly, the cases brought before the Competition  Commission provide the public with much-needed information on exactly when corporate South Africa is being less than frank about the way in which it does business. Such information is an important link in the accountability chain.

The Sasol  price-fixing scandal brings us squarely into the realm of corporate governance and the impact of weak corporate governance on both the market in which a specific product is bought and sold and the impact further down the line on households and consumers.

How accountable are companies to their major stakeholders like employees, shareholders and the broader public?
Do companies see themselves as part of the society in which they operate?

Many times, the answer seems to be a resounding “No”. Out of the  price-fixing scandal and the financial crisis sweeping the world’s economies, lessons must be drawn.

The financial system, like a money merry-go-round, with selective accountability to broader society, is unravelling before our eyes.

The repercussions of a laxity in regulation and monitoring of the corporate sector are now being felt by all and sundry.
Whether it’s the global financial crisis or  price-fixing, it is usually the “little person” who suffers most. Tiger Brands and bread  price-fixing allegations spring to mind here. Such examples of
anti-competitive behaviour provide grist to the mill of trade unions demanding greater corporate
accountability, more regulation and an open discussion on executive remuneration.

Rightly so, for accountability is not something to be extracted from governments and political leadership only, but from those in the corporate world too.

Closer to home, these are some of the arguments the new South African leadership will no doubt hear from the Congress of South African Trade Unions and the South African Communist Party as it debates “fat-cat capitalism” and the structure of the economy.
And so, in a world which has become increasingly borderless, how will Sasol be held to account in
South Africa for its corporate misdeeds in  Europe?

Surely the South African public, a not insignificant stakeholder in Sasol’s interests, has a right to know what happened and whether local markets could be exposed to similar acts of illegality? Should the
tax-payer be demanding that heads roll?

It seems only fair that Sasol faces up to the tough questions at home, if only to serve as a warning to other companies intent on distorting the markets for illegal gain.
l February is manager of Idasa’s Political Monitoring and Information Service.

Economies | souafrz : Southern Africa

SE      In Brief
HD     +EU Fines Paraffin Cartel
WC    220 words
PD     9 October 2008
SN     International Petroleum Finance
SC     IPF
LA      English
CY     (c) 2008 Energy Intelligence Group. All rights reserved

LP
The European Commission has fined nine companies a total of €676 million ($946 million) for operating a European paraffin  wax price-fixing cartel for 13 years. The  fine must be paid within three months.

The companies, which included Eni, Exxon Mobil, Mol, Repsol YPF, RWE, Sasol and Total, were found guilty of participating in the  cartel from 1992 to 2005 in violation of  European Union rules. Royal Dutch Shell, which was also involved in the  price fixing,  had its fine waived after tipping-off the  commission.

TD
The steepest  fine, $445 million, was levied on the  cartel's ring-leader Sasol. Total was  fined $179 million and Exxon $117 million. Sasol's  fine would have been twice as big had it not cooperated with the commission, while Eni's fine was increased by 60% because of its involvement in other cartels dating back to the 1980s, the commission said.

Sasol said it was surprised by the magnitude of the  fine and said it intended to appeal. Eni also said it would appeal the ruling. Exxon said that while it "deeply regretted" its involvement in the cartel and that it would "determine any further actions that need to be taken" after reading the full text of the commission's decision. Total issued a similar statement.

HD      Fines hit EUR 676 M for paraffin wax cartel. WC    116 words

SC     PLRWBM

LP
Nine companies have been fined by the European Commission for a total of EU 676 M for engaging in a paraffin wax cartel. Sasol was given the highest fine at EUR 318.2 M. France-based Total was  fined EUR 128.2 M; ExxonMobil, EUR 83.6 M; and Hansen & Rosenthal, EUR 36 M. RWE, Repsol, MOL, Tudapetrol and ENI were also  fined. ExxonMobil, Repsol and Sasol were awarded a reduction of their fines of 7%, 25% and 50%, respectively, for cooperating with the investigation. A 10th company, Shell,
did not receive a fine for serving as a whistleblower.
Plastics and Rubber Weekly

HD      FACTBOX-EU fines banana importers 60.3 mln euros for cartel

Oct 15 (Reuters) - The European Commission fined banana importers Dole and Weichert a total 60.3 million euros ($82.4 million) on Wednesday for price fixing  in violation of the EU's strict antitrust laws.
Del Monte Inc was held jointly and severally liable by the EU executive for paying the  fine imposed on
Weichert as it controlled the German company at the time of the infringement.

TD
California-based Dole was fined 45.6 million euros, with U.S. company Del Monte and Weichert jointly accountable for 14.7 million euros of the fine.
Dole later said it had not violated  European competition laws, and would appeal against the decision and
fine.

HD     Conference  at the "Parlamentarische Gesellschaft" in Berlin on "European Cartel Prosecution - Legal and Political Question  Marks" on October 14, 2008
WC     1,452 words
PD      15 October 2008
ET      06:33 PM
SN      PR Newswire Europe
SC      TWOTEN
LA      English
CY      Copyright © 2008 PR Newswire Europe Limited. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BERLIN, October 15 /PRNewswire/ --
- Alarmingly Big  Fines and Lack of Rule of Law

TD
- Experts Consider a Great Need for Reforms to Exist in  European Cartel Law

The European Commission takes action against illegal cartel agreements in the EU by imposing spectacularly heavy fines. Recently it hit the so-called Paraffin cartel. The companies concerned have to pay Euro 676 million to the EU treasury. Last year the leading manufacturers of elevators were hit, who together had to pay  fines in an amount of just under  Euro 1 billion. The  Commission imposed a fine of Euro 479 million on ThyssenKrupp alone. Based on experts' observations, sanctions have been substantially increased over the last years. The total amount imposed trebled between 2005 and 2007.

Experts are not only alarmed on account of the amount of the  fines imposed. Rather, many experts believe that the  European cartel law has serious deficiencies. "The demand for a review of the  cartel law system is appropriate and reasonable", conceded even the former Director-General for Competition of the EU Commission, Dr. Alexander Schaub, who discussed the legal and political question marks of the prosecution of cartels under EU law on Tuesday in Berlin in the German Parliamentary Society together with company representatives, expert politicians and jurists who had accepted an invitation from Prof. Dr. Rupert Scholz, an expert on constitutional law, and the ICC's national committees in Germany, Suisse
and Austria.
Proceeding lacks legal standards

The well-known expert on  European Law, Prof. Dr. Jürgen Schwarze, and lawyers from the law firm Gleiss Lutz have closely examined the critical issues of  cartel law in a study. This study provides evidence of numerous weaknesses of the current practice. For a better understanding, one should take a look at the usual procedure. Naturally cartels are discreet associations. The Community's aim is to crack open the illegal groups by way of an immunity rule. The first member of the  cartel who confessed will go unpunished. Other companies incriminating themselves will be granted a deduction from the  fine. The
Commission is free to determine the amount of the  fine at its absolute discretion. The scope of such  fine is up to ten percent of the sales of an undertaking or a group of undertakings. Theoretically large undertakings could thus be sanctioned by  fines in the amounts of billions. The undertakings concerned
are only able to a limited extent to take action against a decision of the  Commission and can file a legal action with the Court of First Instance of the  European Court of Justice in Luxemburg. However, the judges do not re-examine the underlying facts, but only conduct a limited examination without investigating the facts themselves. The same applies to the  European Court of Justice itself as the next and at the same time last instance.

The study produced by Gleiss Lutz finds fault with the unclear legal basis of the law governing  cartel        fines. According to the study, the criteria for determining the amount of  fines are too vague and there is no sufficient legal basis for a liability of the parent undertakings for their subsidiaries. The jurists consider
the immunity rule as well to be questionable, because the other members of the  cartel are factually forced to incriminate themselves in order to escape higher penalties. A further issue with which the experts find fault is the limited judicial control of the decisions. The co-author of the study, Prof. Dr. Jürgen Schwarze, therefore demands a number of reforms of the applicable law. This includes, inter alia, regulations
Page 189 of 191 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
regarding the amount of  fines and the requirement of evidence of an infringement of the  cartel prohibition by specific employees. A further proposal is that compliance programmes by which undertakings intend to prevent infringements should be considered in mitigation. Primarily, however, the analysis advocates a procedure based on the rule of law in which the EU  Commission acts as prosecutor, and a court examines the facts of the individual case and determines the amount of  fines. Mr Schwarze demanded that "a new two-stage procedure should be introduced".
Panel discussion shows need for reforms

The experts largely agree that there is a need for reforms. "We believe that the  European cartel law must be closely examined", Mr Scholz explained. Klaus-Heiner Lehne, too, who is a member of the legal committee of the  European Parliament, is of the view "that there is a need for improvement in terms of
the rule of law". The EPP Member of Parliament pleaded for an independent  cartel authority in  Europe based on the model of the German Federal  Cartel Office and for a discussion about introducing the formation of a  cartel as a criminal offence. In this way offences could also be punished by a prison sentence. This is common practice in the USA. Mr Lehne also considered it important that compliance programmes are taken into account when determining the award of punishment.

The spokesman for legal policy of the CDU/CSU parliamentary group in the German Bundestag, Jürgen Gehb, criticised the lack of a legal basis in the law governing  cartel fines. Mr Gehb said that it is not the amount of the  fines which is at issue, but that such  fines have to be enforced in a manner which is in accordance with the rule of law. Klaus Becher, chief corporate counsel at Daimler Financial Services and deputy chairman of the competition committee of the BDI, i.e. the Federal Association of the German Industry, also complained about legal deficiencies. Mr Becher said that the industry finds it incomprehensible that the Court of First Instance does not conduct any examination of the facts. "This must be changed", demanded Mr Becher.
"Medieval Clan Liability"

Another issue which causes considerable annoyance in the industry is the fact that the parent undertaking is held liable for offences in subsidiaries. "This corresponds to clan liability in the Middle Ages", said the chief corporate counsel of the Total SA group, Dr. Peter Herbel. He said that in criminal law as well, the rule is that it is not an offender's father or mother who is wanted, but the offender himself. Total itself is currently affected by penalty  fines in the Paraffin cartel through a subordinated subsidiary. Mr Herbel complained about a high award of punishment set by the  Commission and said that the  fine was seven times the amount of the company's annual sales and 40 times the amount of its profit. Dr. Klaus Moosmayer, too, considers the  Commission's practice of holding an entire undertaking liable for an infringement of an individual company to be questionable. Mr Moosmayer, who is the head of the compliance programme of Siemens AG, believes that a universal supervision of all employees is impossible in large undertakings. "There will always be inappropriate behaviour", fears Mr Moosmayer,
and the efforts taken by the company groups to prevent infringements should therefore be taken into account. Prof. Dr. Karl Hofstetter, chief corporate counsel and member of the supervisory board of the Swiss Schindler Holding AG, demands in addition that individual offenders be punished. "If the undertaking has done everything to prevent infringements, then the individual offender must be held liable", demanded Mr Hofstetter.

Fines have a deterring effect - but  fine practice should be reviewed

However, the former Director General for Competition of the EU Commission, Mr Schaub, also asked the attendants to have sympathy for the severe course of action taken by the EU Commission. He said that cartel infringements had been regarded as a trivial offence for a long time and had virtually not been prosecuted, and that the  Commission had taken care of this matter. The expert emphasised the effect of big fines as a deterrent. He defended the penalty  fines by stating that "this has resulted in drastic
changes in the undertakings", but that nontheless it has meanwhile become necessary to review the current practice. The Federal  Cartel Office as well considers the  cartel law to be quite a sharp instrument. A representative of the Office emphasised that the immunity rule is "extremely effective" and that the amounts of  fines are a deterrent.

In summary, Prof. Scholz expressed the warning that the efficiency of the measures aimed at combatting cartels must not serve as an authorisation to evade the rule of law.
